fl

117

Cultural Context
•
Indian Cultural In uence on Entrepreneurial Mindset:
Manu’s problem-solving and resourcefulness stem from traditional
Indian values of ‘jugaad’—a Hindi term referring to nding
innovative xes in resource-constrained environments. This cultural
mindset has often been credited with fostering entrepreneurial
abilities in Indian immigrants, who use limited resources to create
scalable, global businesses.
•
Family as a Core Business Unit in Indian Culture: The
role that Rika played in MSI’s success re ects the broader Indian
cultural norm of family-run businesses. In Indian society, families
often work together to create and manage enterprises, and the
balance of roles between husband and wife, as seen with Manu and
Rika, is typical of many Indian entrepreneurial ventures. Rika’s
contributions underscore the important but often underrepresented
role that women play in business, especially in Asian cultures.
•
Cultural Values of Adaptability and Lifelong Learning:
Manu’s belief in continuous learning and adaptability is aligned
with the Indian tradition of lifelong education. In many Eastern
philosophies, learning is seen as a lifelong endeavor, not con ned to
formal education but woven into everyday life experiences. This
cultural approach likely in uenced MSI's adaptability in both hightech and low-tech industries.
•
The American Dream and Immigrant Success: Manu
and Rika’s entrepreneurial success embodies the quintessential
American Dream narrative—where hard work, resilience, and
seizing opportunities allow immigrants to achieve success. This
journey resonates with many immigrants who arrive in the U.S.
seeking better prospects and end up contributing to the country’s
economy through business innovation.

2.2
Starting a Business with
your Spouse
This chapter explores the dynamics of starting a business with a
spouse, using the example of Manu and Rika. Their complementary
strengths, mutual respect, and shared values helped them navigate
both family and business responsibilities. Working from home, they
involved their children in MSI's early stages, balancing their roles as
parents and business owners. The Shahs' commitment to hard work
and building a strong foundation enabled MSI to thrive. They
fostered a family-like culture at MSI, which contributed to its
success. Together, they overcame numerous challenges, focusing on
shared goals and long-term growth.

119

tarting a business with a spouse can be both
rewarding and challenging. Manu and his wife, Rika,
navigated these dynamics with a clear understanding of
their complementary strengths. "Starting a business with
your spouse means you're in it together, 24/7," Manu

2.3
Re e c ting Honor: The
Vietnam War Memorial
Project
This chapter details MSI’s pivotal role in supplying the black
granite for the Vietnam War Memorial in Washington, D.C., a
project that became a turning point for both the company and
Manu Shah. Despite being a small enterprise, MSI took on the
daunting task of sourcing the nest black granite from India.
Manu’s strategic decisions and perseverance allowed MSI to
overcome logistical challenges, including transporting heavy
granite blocks and facing opposition from gures like Ross Perot.
The memorial, completed in 1982, stands as both a symbol of
national remembrance and MSI’s dedication to excellence. The
project was emotionally signi cant for Manu, who attended the
dedication with his family, re ecting on the personal and collective
impact of the war.

fi

fi

fi

fl

fl

127

n the early 1980s, amidst a backdrop of controversy
surrounding the Vietnam War, a monumental project
was conceived to honor those who served and sacri ced.

The Fine Arts Commission in Washington D.C. held a blind
competition to design a memorial that would resonate
deeply with the American public. The winning design, a
striking V-shaped wall proposed by Maya Lin, a young
architecture student of Chinese ancestry from Dayton,
Ohio, was selected unanimously in 1981. Her concept was
simple yet profound: a polished black granite wall inscribed
with the names of over 58,000 servicemen and women
who died in Vietnam, designed to allow visitors to see their
re ections alongside the names, prompting personal
re ection on the war’s impacts.

Despite being a relatively very

was to document the quarrying process and reassure the

fi

fl

129

In the early 1980s, the Vietnam War Memorial in
Washington D.C. became a subject of intense debate, not
only for its symbolic signi cance but also for its design and
material. The original design by Maya Lin, featuring a Vshaped wall inscribed with the names of fallen soldiers, was
intended to be constructed from polished black granite.
This choice of material drew sharp criticism from some,
including businessman Ross Perot, who opposed the use of
black granite, arguing that it symbolized negativity and
defeat. Perot and other critics preferred a more traditional
memorial made from white or lighter-colored stone, which
they believed would better honor the sacri ces of
American soldiers. Despite the controversy, the decision to
use black granite prevailed, and the memorial, completed
in 1982, has since become an iconic and revered symbol of
remembrance and healing, widely praised for its stark,
re ective surface that allows visitors to see their own
re ections among the names of the fallen, thereby creating
a powerful, personal connection to the legacy of the
Vietnam War.

Overcoming Logistical Nightmares
The task was herculean: quarrying, transporting, and
nishing massive blocks of ne black granite without the
aid of modern machinery. Traditional methods were
employed, involving laborious human e ort to move each

fi

ff

fi

fi

fl

130
fl

fi

Ross Perot and the Controversy Over the
Vietnam War Memorial's Black Granite

block inches at a time with shouts of "hi-ho" synchronizing
their painstaking e orts. More than half of the quarried
blocks broke during transport, necessitating the
procurement of signi cantly more material than initially
calculated.
The journey from quarry to the shores of America was
fraught with risks and setbacks. Each block was transported
to Mangalore, a small coastal town in India, where they
were ingeniously unloaded onto ships bound for New York.
Opposition from powerful political gures like Ross Perot,
who derisively called the design a "Black Ditch," and
domestic granite industries lobbying against foreign
materials, added to the project’s controversies.

The Turning Point
The arrival of the granite blocks in New York Harbor in
March 1982 marked a pivotal moment. A CBS news crew
captured the unloading process, broadcasting it during
prime time, which helped to quell some of the public
dissent, and paved the way for the next critical phase—
cutting and polishing the granite slabs in Vermont.
Unfortunately, initial mistakes by the block cutter led to
cracks in the rst four slabs, requiring MSI to urgently
arrange for additional blocks.

Completion and Re ection
By late summer of 1982, all polished slabs were ready and
began to be installed at the memorial site. The meticulous
carving of the names added the nal, somber touch to the

fi

memorial. When news broke on September 23 that the last

achieved—against all odds—began to sink in.
The following Monday, Manu tendered his resignation at
International Truck, a poignant decision marking his full
commitment to MSI. This project was not just MSI’s highestrisk endeavor but also its most prestigious, de ning the
company’s path forward.

Legacy of the Vietnam War Memorial
The Vietnam War Memorial stands today not just as a

capacity to meet extraordinary challenges. For Manu and
Rika, this project underscored their belief in the possible,
no matter how daunting the task. It was a rea rmation of
their entrepreneurial spirit, one that would guide MSI in
the years to come as a leader in sourcing and providing
materials that not only meet but exceed expectations in
both quality and signi cance.

Historical Context
1. Vietnam War and its Impact (1955 1975): The Vietnam
War had a deep and lasting impact on the U.S., resulting in the
deaths of over 58,000 American soldiers and leaving the nation
divided. The Vietnam War Memorial, designed by Maya Lin and
completed in 1982, emerged from this emotional and politically
charged context. The use of black granite, despite initial opposition,
symbolized both mourning and re ection, allowing the memorial to
serve as a place of healing for veterans and families.
2. Memorialization through Architecture (1980s): The
memorial's minimalist design was a radical departure from
traditional war monuments. The use of polished black granite,
which was sourced and provided by MSI, emphasized personal
re ection and public remembrance. This marked a turning point in
memorial design, in uencing future projects worldwide.
Cultural Context
1. Public Controversy over the Memorial’s Design: The
choice of black granite was controversial, as many saw it as a
symbol of defeat rather than honor. However, the decision to
proceed highlighted changing attitudes toward how wars and their
casualties are remembered—moving from glori cation to re ection.
2. Immigrant Contribution to National Legacy: MSI, a
relatively small business started by Indian immigrants, contributed
to a deeply symbolic national monument. This re ects the broader
story of immigrant integration into American society.

2.4
Black Granite in Homes:
Romancing the Black
Beauty
This chapter highlights how MSI revolutionized the use of Indian
black granite in the U.S., moving it beyond memorials and into
homes. Traditionally seen as a somber material, MSI rebranded
black granite, showcasing its durability, versatility, and elegance for
residential and commercial applications. By 1986, after the
successful installation of black granite countertops in a Beverly Hills
home, the trend took o nationwide. MSI’s innovative e orts led to
the popularization of Premium Plus Black Granite and the
introduction of Black Galaxy, making black granite a staple in home
design. Today, MSI remains the largest supplier of black granite in
the U.S.

ff

ff

137

n 1981, MSI was operating from Rika & Manu Shah’s
basement, focusing on growing their business amidst
an expanding black granite market. Black granite was used
in 52 classic memorials globally, including the Vietnam War
Memorial and Bruce Lee Memorial in Washington, D.C.
That year, MSI secured the contract to supply black granite
for the Vietnam Veterans Memorial in Washington, D.C.
Manu and Rika invested their entire savings and worked
tirelessly to ensure the project's success, revolutionizing the
perception and use of Indian black granite in the United
States.
The western world went gaga over the Indian back
granite. If you are surprised to read this, let me surprise
you a bit more! From innovative sculptures to classic
memorials, Indian granite had fascinated the world with its
magni cence. It was time to introduce our black beauty as
the biggest staple for other decorative purposes in the west.
Research and development are one of MSI’s many
strengths. MSI has a tradition of coming up with unusual
ideas that add value at multiple steps, making our products
and services unique. Through MSI, Manu made sure the
black beauty got the attention it deserved.
Creating new selling ideas from limited resources is an
innovation in itself. – Kishor Panchani, Senior Vice
President - Service : IT Infrastructure Team, MSI, Grove,
California

fi

138

Scope For More

Traditionally, black granite was associated with grief and
used for memorials. People feared that using black granite
would make their homes feel and look darker. But MSI
looked at it as a unique product with features that can be
used in many other applications within the home. It took
MSI a while to convince the people that the hard, smooth,
and mirror-like glossy Indian granite was much more than
just a tombstone.
One of the signi cant advantages of black granite is its
strength and durability. It was a great option for high-usage
areas such as kitchen and bathroom countertops. As an
igneous rock formed under extreme pressures, it had the
capability to withstand everyday wear and tear with low
maintenance. It wasn’t easily a ected by heat or scratching.
It had a low water absorption rate, resulting in no stains
and high re ectivity.

ff

fi

fl

139

Moreover, it was incredibly versatile. It was formal as
well as neutral and could suit traditional, transitional, and
contemporary designs. It could easily be incorporated into
any color palette, providing a timeless look.

Leaving No Stone Unturned
In 1986, MSI realized that natural stone for residential
and commercial usage had even larger market potential
and pivoted their e orts towards developing this sector. In
July 1986, when one couple in Beverly Hills chose MSI’s
Premium Plus Black Granite for their kitchen countertop,
the tide turned in favor of this unique stone. People
noticed! Soon many homes in the area wanted to install
black granite. By November, the trend spread to
Manhattan, NY. There was no looking back.
In February 1987, MSI introduced and trademarked Black
Galaxy, another black granite. We also popularized the use
of Premium Plus Black in the United States. In 1997, by
opening a second location in Edison, New Jersey, MSI
became the rst nationwide distributor of natural stones.
Once regarded as a stone associated with death, black
granite was now a coveted design element, used in homes
and o ces: from kitchens and bathrooms to ooring and
facades: “Memorializing Dead person to Serving Living
People”.

ff

fi

ffi

Growing Glory
To date, MSI remains the largest supplier of Black Granite
in the US. Natural stones generally possess a timeless
appeal, but black granite in particular, has remained a
prominent product that hasn’t lost its appeal since its
venture into our living spaces. MSI and Black granite not
only share a history together but also remain strong and
versatile, adapting to the changing trends and testing times.
Historical Context
1. Shift in Material Usage (1980s): In the early 1980s, black
granite was traditionally associated with memorials and somber
spaces. However, MSI's successful marketing of the material for
residential use, starting in Beverly Hills in 1986, coincided with
broader trends of incorporating natural materials into interior
design. This marked a signi cant cultural shift in how consumers
perceived and used black granite, transforming it from a funerary
material to a symbol of luxury and durability in homes.
2. Growth of the U.S. Housing Market (1980s): The U.S.
experienced a housing boom in the 1980s, providing opportunities
for innovative building materials to be incorporated into residential
architecture. MSI's introduction of black granite into homes
coincided with this growing demand for luxury home features.
Cultural Context
1. Cultural Transformation of Design Aesthetics:
Traditionally, black granite was seen as a symbol of grief. MSI's
e orts rebranded it as a sleek, modern material for homes,
re ecting the growing consumer appetite for durable and unique
design choices in the 1980s. This mirrors broader cultural shifts

fi

fl

ff

141

toward minimalism and industrial aesthetics in the interior design
world.
2. Adaptation of Indian Materials to Western Tastes: By
introducing black granite from India into U.S. homes, MSI bridged
two cultures, adapting Indian natural resources to meet Western
design sensibilities. This highlights the globalization of material
sourcing and the blending of aesthetic traditions across continents.

grew into a global powerhouse. Even in times of economic
downturn, Manu's ability to pivot and adapt helped the company
maintain stability and gain market share. By emphasizing
innovation, risk-taking, strategic thinking, and the adoption of new
technologies, Manu demonstrates that the key to entrepreneurial
success lies in being agile, a lesson reinforced by the examples of
pioneers like Alexander Graham Bell and Steve Jobs.

Historical Context
The landscape of entrepreneurship has evolved signi cantly over
the decades, shaped by technological advancements, economic
shifts, and cultural changes. In the late 20th century, the rise of
Silicon Valley and tech innovations have continuously rede ned
industries and challenged businesses to adapt or perish. A notable
example is Kodak, a giant in the analog photography market, which
faced a pivotal challenge with the advent of digital photography
integrated into smartphones. Despite being a pioneer in digital
imaging technolo y, Kodak was slow to transition from its
traditional lm-based business model. This hesitation allowed
competitors to capitalize on the digital photography boom,
eventually leading to Kodak's bankruptcy in 2012. This case
highlights the critical need for companies to adapt to technological
disruptions, regardless of their past successes or market dominance.
During the 1960s and 1970s, when Manu began his
entrepreneurial journey, the global business environment was
marked by rapid industrialization and technological change.
Companies had to be agile to survive and thrive. Manu's ability to
navigate this dynamic landscape by constantly adapting his
strategies is a testament to his entrepreneurial acumen.

Opportunities in Challenges
Manu's journey is replete with instances where his

decision to enter the granite and stone business, a sector
vastly di erent from his engineering background. "I saw an
opportunity in the growing demand for natural stone," he
recalls. This strategic pivot not only diversi ed his business
portfolio but also positioned him as a key player in a
booming industry.

stability and even grow MSI during tough times.
Bill Gates, re ecting on his own entrepreneurial journey,
once said, "Your most unhappy customers are your greatest
source of learning." Manu's approach aligns with this

opportunities for growth and improvement.

The MSI Journey and Milestones
The story of MSI (M S International Inc.) is a testament to
Manu's entrepreneurial vision and adaptability. Founded in

Business: The Cornerstone
of MSI’s Success
This chapter discusses how Manu and Rika made fairness in
business not only an ethical principle but also a key strate y that
fueled MSI's growth and reputation. Their commitment to fairness
shaped relationships with customers, suppliers, and employees,
fostering trust and long-term partnerships. A pivotal moment came
during a transaction in Vermont, where Manu learned the value of
fair pricing as essential for sustainable business relationships. This
approach extended to paying suppliers early, ensuring trust and
quality in their dealings. Their sons, Raj and Rup, further embedded
this fairness philosophy into MSI’s operations, strengthening the
company's global reputation and proving that ethical practices lead
to enduring success in business.

g

152

Learning and Practicing Fairness
The Vermont Incident
A pivotal moment in Manu’s understanding of fairness
occurred during a visit to a customer in Barre, Vermont, in
1980. This incident exempli es how fairness became a
foundational principle for MSI:
Manu was introducing a high-quality red granite from
India to Bert Rouleau, a potential buyer from Vermont.
Impressed by the sample, Bert issued a purchase order for
ve large blocks without mentioning the price. Caught up
in the excitement, Manu initially didn’t notice the omission.
Manu called up Rika to share the big news, and the rst
question Rika asked was “What will be our selling price?”
Manu just realized his oversight and ran up to Bert.

fi

fi

153
fi

fi

ro tability is often prioritized over fairness in the
business world. However, fairness in business
dealings is more than a moral choice; it is a strategic
approach that fosters long-term relationships and
sustainable success. For Manu and Rika, fairness was not
just a personal virtue but a principle they endeavored to
embed deeply within the culture of MSI. This chapter
explores how this commitment to fairness shaped the
company's interactions across all levels—from family
members to international partners—and how it has been a
driving force behind MSI’s reputation and growth.

When asked what the price should be, Bert’s response
became a life-long lesson for Manu in fairness: “Manu, be
fair to us; if you charge too much, you won't have us as a
customer. If you charge too little, you might be out of
business, and all the money we spend promoting this
granite will be wasted. Just be fair.” This interaction
profoundly in uenced Manu's business philosophy,
reinforcing that fairness must be the guiding principle in all
transactions.

Fairness as a Business Strate y
Manu’s takeaway from the Vermont encounter was clear—
fairness is not merely ethical; it's economical. This
principle guided MSI’s pricing strategies with big-box
retailers and other customers. Manu understood that fair
pricing ensured faster inventory turnover, which was more

g

fl

154

pro table in the long run than high markups on slowermoving stock.

Extending Fairness Beyond Transactions
Under Manu’s leadership, MSI adopted a policy of paying
suppliers the day after a container was shipped, well before
the goods were received. This practice not only built
immense trust but also secured MSI’s supply lines, making
it a preferred business partner worldwide. A supplier once
questioned what would happen if substandard goods were
shipped since MSI paid upfront. Manu’s response
highlighted the ethos of MSI: “We might lose money on one
container, but you would lose a lifetime customer.” This
approach has ensured that MSI never received substandard
goods and never lost money due to unfair practices in its
supply chain.

Raj and Rup’s Expansion of the Fairness
Doctrine
Raj and Rup Shah, having observed their parents’
commitment to fairness, have not only continued this
legacy but also expanded it. They ensured that fairness
permeated all aspects of MSI’s operations, from employee
relations to customer engagements and international
dealings. Their leadership reinforced the idea that fairness
and trust are critical to global business success and are
essential for fostering a healthy, productive corporate
environment.

Conclusion: The Enduring Impact of Fairness
Fairness in business, as practiced by MSI, is a testament
to the belief that ethical practices lead to sustainable
success. This approach has helped MSI build and maintain
strong relationships across all levels of operation,
contributing to its reputation as a trustworthy and reliable
partner in the industry. The story of MSI’s commitment to
fairness serves as an inspiring model for businesses
worldwide, proving that success can be achieved without
compromising on core ethical values.
Fairness in business is a foundational principle that
signi cantly contributes to the growth and longevity of a
company. Treating suppliers, employees, and customers
with fairness builds trust, loyalty, and a positive reputation,
which are essential for sustainable success. Fair dealings
with suppliers ensure reliable partnerships and consistent
quality, reducing risks and fostering innovation through
collaborative e orts. Employees who experience fairness in
the workplace are more motivated, engaged, and loyal,
leading to higher productivity and lower turnover rates.
This positive work environment attracts top talent and
cultivates a culture of mutual respect and integrity.
Additionally, fair treatment of customers enhances
satisfaction, trust, and brand loyalty, encouraging repeat
business and positive word-of-mouth referrals. By
embedding fairness in all business practices, companies
can create a virtuous cycle of trust and growth, ensuring
long-term resilience and competitive advantage in the
marketplace.

Culture Shock: How to Maintain the Momentum of
Organizational Culture Change Post Crisis
To exemplify the importance of maintaining a dynamic
and challenging environment, consider the innovative
approach used by Japanese shermen, known for their love
of fresh sh. As the waters close to Japan became depleted,
shermen had to venture further, which led to longer trips
and less fresh sh. Initially, they tried freezing the sh at
sea, but the taste was noticeably di erent. They then
moved to storing sh in tanks, but the lack of movement
resulted in dull, less avorful sh.
The breakthrough came when they added a small shark
to the tank. The shark created a challenge, keeping the sh
active and thus maintaining their freshness. This story
parallels the workplace environment where, without
challenge s, te ams c an become complacent and
unproductive. The "shark" in this metaphor represents new
challenges that keep employees engaged and push them to
innovate and strive for excellence.
Have you realized that some of us are also living in a
pond but most of the time tired and dull ? Basically in our
lives, sharks are new challenges to keep us active. If you are
steadily conquering challenges, you are happy. Your
challenges keep you energized. Don’t create success and

Examples of Leadership at MSI
Commitment to Employee Well-being
Having spent his rst 17 years in the Midwest of America,
Manu witnessed the frequent layo s in the auto and
manufacturing industries. He saw rsthand the devastating
e ects of boom and bust cycles on the workforce. This
experience led him to make a pledge: MSI would not lay o
employees due to a lack of work. Manu believed that
employees were the best assets a company could have,
even if they didn't appear on the balance sheet. He coined
the phrase, "Your pain is our pain - your gain is our gain,"
embodying his belief that the company's success is
intrinsically linked to the well-being of its employees.
Avoiding Cash Transactions
Manu also took a strong stance against cash transactions,
which he likened to DUI for small businesses. He believed
that once hooked on cash transactions, it was not easy to
give them up, and they often led to legal violations for both
the giver and the taker. This principle has guided MSI in
maintaining ethical business practices and building a
reputation for integrity and transparency.

enabled MSI to remain competitive and adaptable in a rapidly
changing market.

Analytical Frameworks and Continuous
Improvement
ARCS Driving Actions from Insights
The ARCS model (Analysis, Recommendations,
Conclusions, and Suggestions) at MSI exempli es how data
is transformed into actionable insights.
•
Analysis: This initial step involves a deep dive into
the raw data collected across various business operations.
Managers and analysts scrutinize the data to detect
patterns, anomalies, trends, and correlations. By examining
the data from multiple dimensions—such as time periods,
product lines, and geographic regions—the team can

ffi

fi

fl

—

171

understand the underlying dynamics of business
operations.
•
Recommendations: Based on the insights gained
from the analysis, the next step is to formulate strategic
recommendations. These are actionable proposals aimed at
exploiting opportunities or mitigating risks identi ed
during the analysis phase. Recommendations are expected
to be practical, grounded in data, and aligned with the
company's strategic objectives.
•
Conclusions: After formulating recommendations,
managers draw conclusions about the overall health and
trajectory of the business. This involves synthesizing the
data and insights into a coherent narrative that can be
understood and acted upon by senior leadership.
Conclusions often highlight the e cacy of current
strategies and validate or challenge existing business
assumptions.
•
Suggestions: The nal step involves suggesting
further actions that could be taken to enhance business
operations. This could include adjustments to existing
procedures, the introduction of new tools or technologies,
or even the recommendation for additional research or
data collection. Suggestions are aimed at fostering
continuous improvement and ensuring the business
remains agile and responsive to market demands.
The ARCS model not only ensures that MSI leverages data
comprehensively but also fosters a culture of informed
decision-making and continuous improvement, keeping the

fi

ffi

fi

172

organization strategically aligned and competitive in a fastevolving market.
This model encourages managers to not only analyze
data but also to make informed recommendations, draw
conclusions, and suggest further actions. This ongoing
cycle ensures continuous improvement and strategic
alignment across the organization.

Beyond the Numbers
Cultivating a Deeper Understanding
MSI fosters an environment where leaders are
encouraged to read "between the lines" of data reports and
graphs to uncover underlying trends and insights. The
mantra, "We know what we know; we don’t know what we
don’t know," prompts a culture of curiosity and continuous
learning, pushing teams to explore beyond the obvious and
consider what additional data might be needed for better
decision-making.

calculated risks supported by safety nets. Throughout MSI's growth,
from its founding to signi cant projects like the Vietnam War
Memorial, Manu and Rika relied on these safety measures to pursue
bold initiatives while protecting the company’s foundation. MSI’s
success is also rooted in nancial prudence, treating safety nets as
essential to balance risks, ensure growth, and maintain long-term
stability.

The Dangers of Bureaucracy as a Safety Net

The MSI Strate y: Focused Expansion and

In the early days of MSI’s expansion, particularly during

and building substantial volume without getting entangled
in a high-stakes battle with local competitors.
This approach requires strategic thinking and awless
execution. By avoiding direct confrontation in saturated
markets, MSI was able to focus on expanding its reach in
less crowded areas. This not only enabled MSI to establish a
strong market presence but also to bene t from predictable
delivery and streamlined operations. The decision to stay
under the radar in certain competitive landscapes allowed
MSI to build its market share e ectively and e ciently,
proving that sometimes strategic restraint can lead to
signi cant advantages.

ceramic tiles, which were familiar and widely accepted.
The turning point came when competitors from Italy and
India began to enter the market with black granite
countertops. As more companies introduced this material,
it started gaining traction among homeowners. Suddenly,
what was once considered exotic became a desirable
showpiece in homes. The competition led to increased
volume, innovations in quarrying and production, and the
advent of containerized cargo, all of which helped to lower
prices and improve availability. Manu quickly realized that
competition was not something to fear but to embrace. It
was competition that helped black granite countertops
become a staple in American homes.
This pattern repeated itself across other product lines.
Between 1987 and 1995, Manu introduced multicolor slates
into the American market. Initially, it was a struggle, but as
more competitors joined the fray, these slates found their
place in homes across the country. The introduction of
travertine from Mexico and Ecuador in 1994 faced a similar
trajectory, but once travertine from Turkey entered the
market, erce competition drove it to become a ooring
staple in American homes. Similarly, Brazil’s Uba Tuba
granite, initially facing tough competition, ultimately
became a go-to material for countertops.

Global Lessons: The Grand Bazaar and Beyond
Manu’s belief in the power of competition was further
solidi ed during a visit to the Grand Bazaar in Istanbul. The
Bazaar, a bustling hub of commerce with around 4,000

shops, exempli ed how competition drives innovation and
growth. Each shop, despite selling similar products, thrived
by nding unique ways to stand out. Manu observed that
competition didn’t just spur businesses to improve; it
fostered collaboration and mutual success among
merchants. This experience rea rmed his conviction that
competition is not something to fear but to embrace.
The lesson Manu gleaned from the Grand Bazaar was that
competition, far from being detrimental, was essential for
driving innovation and fostering growth. Competition
creates an environment where merchants are compelled to
innovate, improve their products, and enhance the
customer experience to stand out. At the same time,
competition drives down prices and increases demand,
ultimately bene ting both businesses and consumers.

Building a Strong Brand Through Competition

Marketing played a crucial role in establishing MSI’s

the go-to brand in every market it entered.

Why Think at All?
Strategic thinking is the ability to see the entire
enterprise, spot trends, understand the competitive
landscape, and lead the business into the future. It involves
using one's mind to consider or reason about something.
Finding time to engage in focused thinking is crucial for
e ective leadership and decision-making.

Levels of Thinking
John Maxwell, author of "How Successful People Think,"
lists several bene ts of what he calls “focused” thinking:
• Harnessing ener y toward a desired goal
• Giving ideas time to develop

Strategic thinking involves di erent levels:
• Level 1: Self-awareness, critical thinking, intellectual
curiosity, and openness.
• Level 2: Exercising good judgment, problem-solving,
decision-making, business acumen, and customer focus.
• Level 3: Successfully creating new and di erent
solutions, dealing with ambiguity, managing innovation,
and perspective thinking.

ine ciencies and slow pace of domestic manufacturing.

How To Shape A Culture Of Innovation
• Build Clarity and Alignment around Innovation:
Aligning our team around a standard de nition of the
term ‘innovation’ is the rst step. Next, we must adopt a
common framework and language around the topic and
ensure everyone understands that innovation is not
simply ‘the act of coming up with new ideas but actually
implementing them after proper testing and validation.

fi

g

fi

ffi

223

• Create Psychological Safety: Looking honestly at
how we react to failure can be a very expressive factor
when innovation is the goal. If the culture of an
organization blames, shames, or punishes, the chances
that people will feel comfortable displaying behaviors
other than vertical compliance are slim to none. Hence a
culture of innovation expects a culture of openness and
freedom of thought.
• Encourage Dialogue: Honest and open dialogue can
only occur once people feel comfortable sharing their
true feelings. Reinforce the behaviors necessary to evolve
toward an innovation culture.
• Challenge Your Assumptions: It’s incredible how
many people operate daily, using an entire set of
unwritten or irrelevant “rules.” Of course, rules are in
place for a reason, but they shouldn’t be accepted blindly
without understanding the “why” behind those rules. A
practice is not necessarily correct just because it has
“always been done this way.” When the factors
contributing to the origin of the practice are no longer
relevant, the process itself should be subject to
improvement.
• Embrace Diversity: Bringing together a diverse
workforce can amplify our ability to innovate. Research
suggests that having diverse experiences, perspectives,
and backgrounds is crucial to innovation and developing
new ideas.

Fear of Failures
This chapter emphasizes Manu Shah's belief that failure is a
powerful teacher when paired with the right safety nets, which can
drive both learning and growth. Manu and Rika took calculated
risks when founding MSI, ensuring safeguards were in place to
mitigate potential losses. The chapter explores how they learned
from failures in partnerships and acquisitions, helping steer MSI
toward sustainable growth. It also highlights the introduction of

nancial metrics, without diluting company equity.

ff

2.15

anu believes that failure is the best coach for
learning and evolving. From a young age,
children learn crucial lessons about trying, failing, and
improving. At 9 10 months old, a child observes others
standing and walking, tries to do the same, fails, improves,
and eventually stands up, bringing joy to their parents. This
process forms clusters of neurons in the brain, teaching the
child valuable lessons in resilience. Parents ensure a "safety
net" to prevent serious injury, a principle applicable to any
risk in life or business.

Lifelong Lessons
The lessons of trying, failing, and improving continue
throughout life in activities like walking, running, bicycling,
and swimming. However, many adults become fearful of
taking risks due to the fear of failure.

Manu and Rika's Risks
Manu and Rika took signi cant risks with a safety net in
place. During the auto industry layo s in late 1974, they
decided to always have a safety net. Although Manu was
not laid o , they founded MSI to supplement their income.
Manu continued his job at International Truck in Fort
Wayne, Indiana, as a safety net while MSI grew. When the
export of chips and microprocessors shifted to the Far East,
MSI was already importing granite blocks. Manu quit his job
as a Senior Project Engineer only after the Vietnam War
Memorial was completed in September 1982.

Facing Failures
Fear is the worst enemy of any journey. Despite

This chapter emphasizes the importance of learning from
failures, maintaining a safety net, and making strategic
decisions to ensure sustainable growth and success.
To solve feeing of ownership, MSI created Phantom Stock
– Ownership plan starting 2002.

Phantom Stock Plan: A Win-Win Solution for
Private Companies Like MSI
For private companies like MSI, o ering traditional stock
options to employees may not always be the most viable or
e ective incentive. In contrast, Phantom Stock Plans
provide a highly exible, valuable, and risk-free option for
rewarding key leaders, supervisors, and high-performing
employees. Phantom stock is essentially a form of deferred
compensation where employees receive cash bonuses that
mimic the bene ts of stock ownership—without the
complexities of issuing actual shares or diluting equity.

What Is a Phantom Stock Plan?
A Phantom Stock Plan grants employees "phantom" or
virtual shares, which are tied to the nancial performance
of the company. The employees don’t own actual stock, but
their rewards are linked to the value of the company, just as
if they were real shareholders. At MSI, the value of
phantom stock can be tied to key nancial metrics, such as
revenue growth and gross pro t margin. Employees are
rewarded based on the company's performance over time.

fi

fi

ff

fi

fi

fl

ff

231

Employees in the phantom stock plan receive a payout
equivalent to the value of the virtual shares, typically after
a vesting period. MSI’s plan, for example, has a three-year
vesting period, after which employees receive guaranteed
cash payouts each year based on the growth and
pro tability of the company.

Advantages of Phantom Stock Plans
Phantom stock plans o er a range of bene ts for both
employees and the company, making them a perfect t for
a private company like MSI:
Alignment with Company Success: Since the value of
phantom stock is tied directly to MSI's revenue growth and
pro t margins, employees are motivated to contribute to
the company’s overall success. The better MSI performs,
the higher the payout for employees.
Guaranteed Cash Out: One of the key bene ts of MSI’s
Phantom Stock Plan is that it o ers a guaranteed cash-out
option after the initial three-year period. Employees don’t
need to worry about market volatility or waiting for an IPO
or acquisition to realize value from their shares. This
certainty provides a clear and motivating nancial
incentive.
No Equity Dilution: As a private company, MSI is able to
keep full control of its ownership structure. Since phantom
stock is not actual equity, there’s no need to issue new
shares, preventing the dilution of ownership for existing
shareholders.

fi

fi

fi

fi

ff

ff

fi

fi

232

Retention Tool: The three-year vesting period serves as
an excellent employee retention tool, especially for leaders
and supervisors. By o ering a deferred payout, employees
have an incentive to stay with the company longer to fully
realize the bene ts of the plan.
F l ex i b i l i t y: P h a n t o m s t o c k p l a n s a re h i g h ly
customizable. MSI can adjust the terms based on the
company's performance metrics, such as tying payouts to
revenue growth or gross pro t margin, or even providing
additional rewards based on individual performance.

A Focus on Long-Term Growth
By implementing a Phantom Stock Plan tied to metrics
like revenue growth and gross pro t margin, MSI creates an
ecosystem where employees are rewarded for their
contributions to the long-term growth and pro tability of
the company. This structure ensures that leaders and
supervisors remain focused on driving sustainable success
and are incentivized to perform their best year after year.
Phantom stock plans, with their guaranteed payouts and
alignment with company performance, o er an ideal
solution for private companies like MSI to incentivize and
reward top talent without the challenges of traditional
stock options or equity dilution. They help build a culture
of long-term commitment, shared success, and nancial
security for the company and its employees.

individuals are open-minded, resourceful, and quick

Overcoming a Ransomware Attack: Rapid
Response and Recovery
In December Xmas day 2019, MSI faced a severe
ransomware attack that threatened the security of our data
and operations. While such attacks often paralyze
companies for weeks or even months, our team’s
preparedness and swift action ensured a rapid recovery.
Working tirelessly day and night, we managed to restore
our systems in less than three days. This remarkable feat
was a testament to our robust risk management and
mitigation processes, which allowed us to bounce back
quickly and continue serving our clients without signi cant
disruption. One of very few companies recovered within 48
Hours without paying any Ransom!

private presents challenges like limited access to capital and
reduced visibility, MSI has overcome these through customer
loyalty, nancial discipline, and solid internal processes, ensuring
its stability even during economic downturns.

2.21
Leadership - Hungry for
Problem Solving
Leadership is not just about vision; it is about solving problems
before they escalate into crises. Great leaders anticipate challenges
and act swiftly, understanding that postponing issues only makes
them worse. Throughout history, from Franklin D. Roosevelt's New
Deal during the Great Depression to Nelson Mandela's reconciliation
e orts in post-apartheid South Africa, e ective leaders have tackled
problems head-on. In the business world, Steve Jobs at Apple
streamlined product lines and reignited innovation by addressing
unsold inventory and ine ciencies. At MSI, challenges like senior
leadership issues, unsold inventory, or operational ine ciencies
can’t be left to linger. Managing by crisis is unsustainable; instead,
leaders must identify and resolve one major problem each week,
along with a few smaller ones, to stay ahead. As Peter Drucker
noted, the danger lies not in wrong answers, but in asking the wrong
questions—leaders must confront the tough issues to guide their
teams to success. Ultimately, leadership is about problem-solving;
without problems to solve, leadership becomes irrelevant.

ffi

ff

ffi

ff

276

eadership isn’t just about having a vision; it’s about
taking action when things go wrong. All too often,
unresolved problems—whether large or small— can fester
and grow, creating crises that should never have existed in
the rst place. A good leader recognizes that postponing an
issue doesn't make it disappear. In fact, it makes it worse.
As the ancient Chinese proverb says, "The best time to
plant a tree was 20 years ago. The second-best time is now."
This same principle applies to leadership—solving problems
at the earliest opportunity ensures future stability and
growth.
Throughout history, great leaders have distinguished
themselves by their ability to confront and resolve
problems. Consider Abraham Lincoln during the American
Civil War. Faced with the monumental challenge of keeping
the Union together, Lincoln made the di cult decision to
issue the Emancipation Proclamation, a move that not only
addressed the issue of slavery but also strengthened the
Union’s moral cause. In doing so, he confronted a lingering
problem that had divided the country for decades, securing
both a military and moral victory.
In the world of technolo y, Steve Jobs’ leadership at
Apple provides another compelling example. Upon his
return to the company in 1997, he faced a bloated product
line and unsold inventory clogging up Apple’s supply chain.
Rather than allowing these issues to drag the company
down, Jobs made swift decisions to streamline the product
lineup, cutting away excess and focusing on innovation. His
problem-solving approach was a key factor in Apple’s
resurgence as one of the world’s most valuable companies.

ffi

g

fi

277

At MSI, There are similar patterns. Sometimes, a senior
leader needs to be demoted or let go for the good of the
organization. Unsold inventory takes up valuable space in
our warehouses, and habitual tardiness from otherwise
good associates can set a bad example for others. Other
times, it’s operational costs spiraling out of control, a
salesperson’s refusal to promote new products, or ongoing
space and tra c issues that cause headaches at Warehouse
& su er Customer Experience. Whatever the problem, it’s
the leader’s job to identify it, confront it, and solve it.
Managing by crisis is not sustainable. Crisis management
often results from letting problems linger too long, waiting
until they erupt into something far bigger than they need to
be. As leaders, we must avoid falling into this trap. The
solution? Each week, identify at least one major problem
that has been lingering for too long. In addition, pick a few
smaller issues and resolve them. This consistent approach
ensures that we stay ahead of potential crises and maintain
a proactive, rather than reactive, mindset.

Modern Leadership: Act or Manage by Crisis?
In today’s fast-paced world, leaders face a myriad of
challenges—from operational ine ciencies to sta ng
issues, from unsold inventory clogging up valuable
warehouse space to managing associates with chronic
lateness. These are the kinds of problems that don’t
disappear with time but instead compound until they
demand urgent attention. Some leaders manage by crisis,
waiting until the situation is dire before acting. But the best

ffi

ffi

ffi

ff

278

leaders know that the key is to regularly address problems
before they balloon into larger issues.
Whether it’s controlling operational costs, dealing with a
senior leader whose performance has slipped, or
addressing logistical issues like space and tra c, leaders
must make decisions. A proactive leader should aim to
solve at least one major lingering problem each week and a
few smaller ones. This steady approach ensures smooth
operations and reinforces the message that leadership is
about action, not avoidance.
As Peter Drucker, the father of modern management,
once said, "The most serious mistakes are not being made
as a result of wrong answers. The true dangerous thing is
asking the wrong questions." In other words, it is the
leader’s responsibility to ask the tough questions and then
take action to resolve the answers—no matter how
uncomfortable they may be.
Ultimately, all leaders must understand their main job: to
lead through solving problems. Without problems to solve,
a leader’s role becomes irrelevant. It is through addressing
these challenges, week after week, that leaders truly
demonstrate their worth and set their organizations on a
path to success. So, the next time a problem arises, don’t
wait for it to become a crisis. Solve it, and lead your team to
a better, more secure future.

The Role of Problem Solving in Leadership
At its core, leadership is
problem-solving. If there
are no problems, why do
we need a leader?
Leadership is about more
than holding a title—it’s
about creating solutions,
ensuring the organization
remains agile, and driving
continuous improvement.
The strength of leadership
is not in avoiding
challenges but in facing them directly and consistently.
In conclusion, whether rooted in history, technolo y, or
cultural wisdom, the lesson remains the same: great
leaders solve problems. They act with foresight, courage,
and integrity, knowing that their primary role is to guide
their team or organization through the challenges of today
while preparing for tomorrow’s opportunities. So, when a
problem arises, take that rst step—lead, and solve.

Historical Context: Leadership Through Crisis
In history, some of the most revered leaders were those who faced
problems head-on rather than allowing them to fester. One vivid
example is Franklin D. Roosevelt during the Great Depression. When
FDR assumed the presidency in 1933, the U.S. was reeling from a
crippling economic downturn. Rather than delaying, he took
immediate action, launching the New Deal, a series of programs
and reforms that directly addressed unemployment, nancial
instability, and social welfare. His proactive stance is a prime
example of leadership not waiting for problems to turn into fullblown crises, but instead tackling them early with decisive action.
In the same vein, Nelson Mandela’s approach to leadership
during South Africa's transition from apartheid is equally
instructive. Mandela faced decades of racial division, political strife,
and the deep-rooted problems of inequality. Rather than managing
by crisis, he proactively worked to heal a fractured nation through
reconciliation, even when others advocated for retribution. By
addressing the core issue—division—Mandela built a lasting legacy of
unity and peace.
Technological Context: Leading with Innovation

fi

281

In the technolo y world, leaders like Je Bezos exemplify how
problem-solving can steer companies toward success. In the early
days of Amazon, unsold inventory and ine ciencies in the supply
chain were critical problems. Bezos didn’t let these problems
stagnate. Instead, he embraced innovative solutions like the
creation of automated warehouses and the integration of advanced
logistics systems. By addressing these challenges head-on, Amazon
became a logistical powerhouse, setting new standards in ecommerce and delivery. This is a prime example of how
technological leadership involves not just reacting to problems but
anticipating and solving them before they become crises.
Cultural Context: Problem Solving as an Ethical Responsibility
Culturally, di erent societies o er diverse perspectives on
leadership and problem-solving. In Japanese culture, for instance,
the concept of Kaizen, or continuous improvement, has long been a
guiding principle in business and management. This philosophy
suggests that incremental problem-solving daily—however small—
leads to long-term organizational health. Toyota’s famous Just-inTime manufacturing system exempli es this approach. By
continuously addressing production problems and ine ciencies as
they arose, Toyota revolutionized global manufacturing practices,
showcasing how consistent, proactive problem-solving leads to
technological and organizational breakthroughs.
Another cultural perspective can be found in ancient Indian
philosophy, which emphasizes the importance of duty (Dharma).
Leaders, according to the Bhagavad Gita, must ful ll their
responsibilities without attachment to personal gain. This cultural
wisdom reinforces that leaders are ethically bound to act, especially
when they see issues or challenges in their path. Leadership in this
sense is about doing what is right—solving problems when they arise
and not postponing the inevitable.

Stone Age to Silicon Age: A Timeline of
Progress
Natural stone's journey with humanity began over 2.5
million years ago, marking the commencement of the Stone
Age, a period that lasted until the advent of metal use.
Stone was rst employed as basic tools and gradually found
its place in the construction of shelters and complex
structures.

fi

285

• Early Tools and Weapons: The initial interaction

the groundwork for human advancement.
• Discovery of Fire: Around 400,000 years ago, stone
tools were instrumental in one of humanity's most
signi cant discoveries— re. This development not only
provided warmth and a means to cook food but also
protection from predators.

Architectural and Cultural Milestones
As societies evolved, so did the applications of natural
stone, which played a central role in the construction of
some of the world's most iconic monuments and buildings.
• Agricultural Tools: About 12,000 years ago, as
humans settled into agricultural communities, stone
tools were developed for farming, enhancing food
production and stability.
• The Wheel: The invention of the wheel from stone
materials revolutionized transportation and machinery,
facilitating trade and communication.
• Monumental Constructions: From the majestic
pyramids of E ypt built of limestone to the grand
Colosseum of Rome crafted with travertine, natural stone
has been a favored material for monumental architecture
due to its durability and beauty.

A New Vision: Granite in American Kitchens

3.5
The Underground Economy
Home Improvement and
Remodeling
This chapter explores how MSI navigates the fragmented home
improvement industry by adhering to ethical business practices,
even in regions where competitors operate within informal
economies. Despite potential short-term pro t losses, MSI prioritizes
integrity, safety, and transparency, refusing to compromise on
worker safety and regulatory compliance. This ethical approach has
sometimes limited MSI's market share in areas like California and
developing countries, but it underscores the company's commitment
to long-term, responsible business practices, setting a high standard
for the industry.

fi

318

When a home or condominium changes hands—whether
through the sale of an existing property or the purchase of
a new one—homeowners in the U.S. typically invest
between 5% to 20% of the property’s value on upgrades
and renovations. These improvements are often
concentrated in key areas such as the kitchen, master
bedroom, and bathrooms, where modernization e orts
focus on elements like kitchen cabinets, countertops,
ooring, lighting, and appliances. In suburban metro areas,
Big Box Retailers like Home Depot, Lowe’s, F&D, and
Menard’s dominate the market, capturing nearly 40% of
the business through their extensive network of
contractors.

ff

fi

fi

319
fl

fl

he
home
improvement and
r e m o d e l i n g i n d u s t r y,
particularly in the United
States, is a complex and
fragmented landsc ape.
This complexity is further
magni ed in developing
countries around the
world, where the market
dynamics are often
in uenced by a myriad of

However, the path to completing these home
improvements is rarely straightforward. In North America,
one signi cant aspect of remodeling—countertop
installation—illustrates the challenges within this market.
The cost to homeowners for a simple $10 per square foot
slab of material can escalate to around $45 per square foot
when installation fees and additional sales taxes are
included. This steep markup is not just a re ection of labor
costs; it also reveals the undercurrents of an informal,
underground economy that thrives on avoiding taxes and
regulatory oversight.

The Underground Economy: A Double-Edged
Sword
The underground economy in the home improvement
industry is particularly prevalent in densely populated
areas undergoing rapid change, such as North Hollywood in
Southern California, as well as major urban centers like
Chicago, Brooklyn, Queens, and Dallas. In these regions,
cash transactions to avoid sales tax and under-the-table
payments to workers are common practices. This system
allows some unethical contractors to circumvent wage
taxes, worker’s compensation costs, and overtime
premiums, creating a shadow economy that signi cantly
undermines legitimate businesses.
While these practices might o er short-term nancial
advantages for some contractors and homeowners, they
come with substantial long-term risks. The avoidance of
taxes and regulatory costs not only leads to a signi cant
loss of revenue for local governments but also perpetuates

fi

fi

fi

fl

ff

fi

320

a cycle of unlicensed work and inadequate safety measures.
This environment increases the risks for both workers and
homeowners, who may nd themselves without recourse if
something goes wrong.
For MSI, a company that has always prioritized ethical
business practices, the decision to steer clear of high-risk
markets where the underground economy is rampant has
been a deliberate one. While this choice has sometimes
meant sacri cing potential market share in regions like
Mexico, India, and Turkey, it underscores MSI’s unwavering
commitment to integrity and regulatory compliance.

The Human Cost of the Underground Economy
Beyond the nancial implications, the underground
economy has a profound human cost, particularly for
undocumented workers in the home nishing industry. In
regions like Southern California, these workers often
operate in hazardous conditions without proper protective
measures. The lack of safety equipment, such as N95
masks, exposes them to severe health risks like silicosis—a
debilitating lung disease caused by inhaling ne silica
particles. Silicosis is not just a risk; it is a reality for many
workers in developing countries like China, Mexico, and
Brazil, where safety regulations are lax or poorly enforced.
In the U.S., the situation is equally dire for undocumented
workers, who have limited access to healthcare and legal
protections.
These workers, often employed under the table, face a
grim reality. They work in unsafe environments with little
to no recourse if they are injured or fall ill. Without the
protection of legal status, many are forced to continue

fi

fi

fi

fi

fi

321

working in dangerous conditions, unable to voice their
concerns or seek redress. When health issues arise, their
only option may be to seek compensation through legal
channels, a process that is often lengthy, stressful, and
fraught with uncertainty. MSI’s refusal to participate in the
underground economy highlights the company’s broader
commitment to addressing these issues, ensuring that its
business practices do not contribute to such exploitation.

MSI’s Ethical Stance and Strategic Decisions
The decision by MSI to avoid engaging with the
underground economy has had far-reaching implications
for the company’s market position. While some
competitors may have gained market share by cutting
corners, MSI has remained steadfast in its commitment to
legal and ethical business practices. This approach, led by
the Shah family, has ensured that MSI remains compliant
with all legal requirements, even when it means foregoing
potential pro ts in high-risk areas.
In California, particularly in high-cost housing markets,
MSI’s ethical stance has sometimes resulted in a smaller
market share compared to companies willing to engage in
questionable practices. However, the company’s leadership
has no regrets about this approach. For MSI, maintaining
integrity and adhering to ethical standards is not just a
business decision—it is a core value that de nes the
company’s identity.

The Partnership Philosophy
MSI’s approach to supply chain management is based on

Understanding the nancial landscapes in countries like

Recognizing that challenges such as natural disasters,

partner, reinforcing the idea that suppliers’ challenges are

The Impac t of G eneralized System of
Preferences (GSP)
A pivotal development during this period was the
implementation of the Generalized System of Preferences
(GSP), a trade program designed to promote economic
growth in the developing world by providing preferential
duty-free entry for up to 4,800 products from 129
designated bene ciary countries and territories. The
reduction or elimination of tari s under GSP meant that
products essential for home surfacing, such as tiles and
natural stone, could be imported into the United States at
signi cantly lower costs.

Globalization and the Rise of Containerized
Cargo: A Revolution in World Trade
The story of globalization is deeply intertwined with the
development of containerized cargo, a transformative
innovation that has reshaped world trade and supply
chains over the last century. For MSI and its global
operations, containerization has played a pivotal role in
expanding their product reach across continents, ensuring
that goods manufactured in the most remote parts of the
world can be delivered seamlessly to the doorsteps of
American homes and businesses.

The Origin of Containerized Cargo
Containerization was rst introduced in 1956 by an
American trucker and entrepreneur, Malcolm McLean,
who invented the standardized shipping container. Before
this innovation, cargo was loaded and unloaded piece by
piece, a slow, labor-intensive, and costly process. McLean’s

ff

g

fi

346

idea of a metal box
that could be stacked,
transported by truck,
ship, and train
re vo l u t i o n i z e d t h e
shipping industry. The
invention
of
containerized cargo
not only reduced
shipping costs
drastically but also
made international
trade much faster,
s a f e r, a n d m o r e

e cient.

By the 1970s, container ships became a global standard,
leading to the creation of an intricate web of shipping
routes and ports that today facilitate 90% of the world's
trade. Global trade, especially between developing and
developed countries, surged, and companies like MSI
seized this opportunity to connect rural producers to
global markets.

How Cargo Moves: From a Small Village in
North India to Big Box Stores in America
The journey of a product from a rural village in India to
an American big-box store, like Home Depot, is a
sophisticated and well-orchestrated process of logistics and
technolo y that epitomizes the marvels of containerized
cargo.

g

ffi

347

1. From Village to Regional Hub
The journey begins in a small village in northern India
where granite slabs, tiles, or other MSI products are
manufactured or sourced. After being processed or mined
locally, the products are transported by truck or smaller
vehicles to a regional hub, where they are consolidated and
packaged into shipping containers.
2. Rail Transport to Major Port (Mundra)
Once the goods are packaged, the containers are loaded
onto trains that head to Mundra Port, India’s largest
private port. Mundra, located on the western coast of India,
is a critical gateway for international trade. The containers,
now sealed and standardized, make this journey e ciently,
ensuring minimal handling of the cargo.
3. Port to Port: Mundra to U.S. East and West Coasts
At Mundra Port, the containers are transferred onto
large container ships that travel across the Indian Ocean,
the Arabian Sea, and eventually through the Suez Canal to
the U.S. east or west coasts. Major ports such as Los
Angeles, Long Beach, Newark, and Savannah receive
the containers, where they are unloaded onto rail cars for
further inland distribution.
4. Train Transport to Distribution Centers and
Stores
From the U.S. ports, the containers are transferred to
trains that carry them thousands of miles across the
country. Some may head directly to large distribution
centers, while others go straight to retailers. For example, a
container carrying MSI products may arrive in the Midwest,

ffi

348

from where it is distributed to as many as 2,000 big-box
stores such as Home Depot, Lowe’s, and Menard’s.
5. Last-Mile Delivery
Once the containers reach the regional distribution
hubs, the goods are sorted, repackaged if necessary, and
sent to individual stores or directly to customers. This nal
leg of the journey, called **last-mile delivery**, ensures that
the product arrives at its destination promptly, completing
a journey that spans thousands of miles and multiple
modes of transport.

The Impact of Containerization on World
Trade
Containerization has been one of the greatest drivers of
globalization, fundamentally changing how goods are
produced, transported, and consumed. Here's how:
E ciency and Cost Reduction:
Containerized shipping has made global trade cheaper
by reducing the labor and time involved in moving goods.
Ports can now handle thousands of containers in a day,
making global supply chains faster and more responsive to
demand.
Standardization:
The standardized sizes of containers (20-foot and 40foot containers) mean that products can be seamlessly
transferred between ships, trucks, and trains without
repackaging, signi cantly reducing the risk of damage and
theft.

fi

fi

ffi

Global Integration of Economies:
Containerization has played a critical role in the rise of

one part of the world, assembled in another, and sold in yet
another part of the world. This has integrated developing

incomes, and boosting economic growth.
Scale and Reach:
Globalization, powered by containerization, has
allowed companies like MSI to source materials from rural
areas in countries like India, China, Brazil, and Turkey and
deliver them to consumers in America. This ability to scale
operations globally has made MSI a leader in the industry
and a key player in global trade.

The Global Impact of Containerized Cargo:
Jobs and A ordable Goods

Creating Jobs in Rural Areas
Before the advent of containerization, rural areas in
developing countries often struggled to connect with the
global economy due to ine cient, costly, and unreliable
shipping methods. Containerization changed this by
enabling companies to ship goods from the remotest parts
of the world directly to international markets, providing
rural economies with new avenues for growth.
For MSI, which sources natural stone, tiles, and other
surfacing materials from 35 countries, containerized
shipping has been the cornerstone of their ability to
integrate rural producers into the global market. Today, in
2024, MSI supports over 400,000 jobs, with more than
75% of those jobs in rural areas. By partnering with local
quarries, factories, and artisans in countries such as India,
Brazil, Turkey, and China, MSI has enabled rural
communities to participate in the global economy,
providing stable employment, improved infrastructure,
and higher living standards.

Boosting Rural Economies and Enhancing
Livelihoods

these rural areas to become key players in global supply

rural areas. The ability to transport large quantities of
materials through standardized containers allowed for

ffi

economies of scale, which in turn generated more jobs in

and capacity to provide consistent employment. This

Lowering Costs for Consumers
Containerized cargo has also dramatically reduced the
cost of shipping goods, leading to lower prices for
consumers. By standardizing the way products are
transported and reducing the time and labor involved in
loading and unloading cargo, containerization has slashed
shipping costs. For MSI, this e ciency means that the
company can move vast quantities of goods at lower perunit costs, passing the savings onto consumers.
With its strategic use of containerization, MSI has been
able to make products such as granite, porcelain tiles, and
quartz countertops more accessible and a ordable to a
broader audience. As a result, homeowners and businesses
can now enjoy high-quality, durable materials at prices that
were previously unattainable. In the U.S., MSI products
have become a staple in home improvement projects, with

stone more accessible to middle-class families.

Transforming Interviews

This chapter explains how Manu Shah at MSI rede nes the

fi

358

t M S International, Inc. (MSI), the interview
process deviates from the conventional script of
rigid questions and structured assessments. Under Manu
Shah's leadership, the focus shifts from scrutinizing past job
experiences to understanding the person behind the
resume. This chapter explores Manu's unique approach to
interviews, which prioritizes comfort and conversation to
uncover a candidate's true potential, adaptability, and t
within MSI's dynamic culture.

The Art of Conversation in Interviews
Creating a Relaxed Environment
Manu Shah believes
that the best way to
gauge a candidate's
potential is not
through rehearsed
answers but through
s p o n t a n e o u s
conversation. By
discussing topics
unrelated to the job,
such as tra c, local

fi

ffi

359

helps break down barriers and nerves that often

Observing Beyond the Answers
Seeking Traits Not on the Resume
During these conversations, Manu is less interested in the

observing their:
• Adaptability: How well they shift from casual banter
to more profound topics of discussion.
• Inquisitiveness: Their willingness to ask questions or
their curiosity about new subjects.
• Technological Engagement: Enthusiasm for using or
understanding new technologies, which indicates an
openness to innovation.
• Positive Attitude: Their ability to maintain positivity
or forward-thinking in their storytelling and responses.

Vision and Aspiration
Understanding Candidates' Future Goals
Rather than focusing solely on what candidates can o er

3.11
MSI's No Layo s Policy

A Commitment to Stability
This chapter highlights MSI's "No Layo s for Lack of Work"
policy, established by Manu and Rika Shah in response to Manu's
personal experience during the 1970s auto industry recession. The
policy re ects a deep commitment to employee security,
emphasizing long-term well-being over short-term nancial gains.
MSI upholds this principle through careful planning, nancial
discipline, and humility, fostering loyalty and protecting talent.
This approach has enabled MSI to sustain growth and remain
resilient, positioning the company as a model for stability during
economic challenges.

fi

ff

ff

n the late 1970s, the American auto industry was hit
hard by a severe recession, leading to widespread
layo s that shook the stability of countless families. Manu
Shah, then working as an engineer in Fort Wayne, Indiana,
experienced this turbulent period rsthand. On a Friday
morning in 1975, Manu was handed a pink slip—his notice of
layo . However, by the end of that same day, his
exceptional expertise in automation and testing spared him
from unemployment. While Manu's job was saved, he
witnessed over 200 of his colleagues, many of them
engineers and designers, being let go. The human cost of
these layo s—disrupted families, nancial insecurity, and
emotional turmoil—left a profound impact on Manu and his
wife, Rika.
It was during this period of uncertainty that Manu and
Rika made a pledge to themselves: if they were to build
their own company, layo s would be o the table, no
matter the circumstances. This personal vow laid the
foundation for MSI's enduring policy of "No Layo s for Lack
of Work," a policy that has stood strong for 49 years.

The Historical Context: The Recession of the
1970s
The 1970s were a tumultuous time for the American
economy, marked by stag ation, high unemployment, and
an ener y crisis that severely impacted the manufacturing
sector, particularly the auto industry. Companies across the
country resorted to mass layo s as a means of survival. The
widespread economic uncertainty created a ripple e ect,

Indiana, the heart of manufacturing, the human toll of
these layo s was evident, with families facing nancial
ruin, children uprooted from their schools, and entire
communities struggling to cope.
Manu's personal brush with unemployment during this
period was not just a professional challenge but a deeply
human experience. It opened his eyes to the real cost of
layo s—not just for the individuals directly a ected but for
their families, their communities, and the economy.

The True Cost of Layo s
While many companies see layo s as a necessary evil to
cut costs during tough times, the Shah family has always
viewed them as a last resort—one that comes with
signi cant, often overlooked, expenses. The nancial costs
to a company can be substantial, including severance pay,
extended healthcare bene ts for up to 12 months, and the
reduced productivity that comes with reassigning work to
remaining employees. Training or retraining employees to
take on additional duties can further strain resources.
Beyond the immediate nancial burden, layo s can lead
to long-term consequences that undermine the very
survival of a company. Downsizing often triggers a wave of
voluntary departures, as remaining employees, fearing for
their own job security, seek more stable opportunities
elsewhere. This attrition can drain a company of its most
valuable talent. Additionally, layo s can increase a
company’s unemployment insurance tax rates and expose

Forced to relocate to nd new employment, families are
uprooted, with children having to adapt to new schools and
spouses needing to nd new jobs. Selling a home becomes
increasingly di cult in a market ooded with unemployed
workers, leading to nancial and emotional stress that can
take years to recover from.

MSI’s Enduring Commitment
Over the decades, MSI has weathered numerous
economic storms, including rece ssions, market
uctuations, and industry shifts. Through it all, the
company has remained steadfast in its commitment to
avoid layo s, driven by the belief that employees are the
most valuable asset a
company possesses.

No Layo Policy: The Pillars of Planning,
Discipline, and Humility
Implementing a no-layo policy, as MSI has done for

3.12
Navigating Employee
Termination at MSI
This chapter discusses the careful and sensitive approach to
employee termination at MSI, a company committed to avoiding
layo s due to lack of work. Firing for cause (e.g., safety violations or
substance abuse) follows clear HR protocols, while performancerelated terminations require a more sensitive, thorough process.
MSI emphasizes respect, dignity, and ethical considerations during
terminations, ensuring they align with the company’s values. By
acknowledging the Peter Principle—where employees may reach
their competency limit—MSI frames terminations as opportunities
for employees to nd better-suited roles, turning a di cult situation
into a constructive transition.

t M S International,

F i r i n g f o r C a u s e : C l e a r Po l i c i e s a n d
Procedures
Handling Clear Violations
At MSI, terminations for cause are managed by Human
Resources and are relatively straightforward. Causes for
dismissal might include DUI, unsafe operation of
equipment, chronic tardiness, substance abuse,
harassment, theft, or misuse of company property. In such
cases, HR typically provides warnings followed by
termination if the employee does not improve. These

consequences. Manu believes that although tough, these

Thorough Approach
When Expectations Aren't Met
More challenging than ring for cause is terminating an
employee for failing to meet job expectations. This
situation requires careful documentation and a sensitive
approach. Manu emphasizes that such terminations are
akin to removing a slow-growing cancer that can in uence
and demoralize the rest of the team. The process typically
involves a meeting with HR present, where Manu
acknowledges the employee's talents but explains that they
may be better suited in a di erent role or organization " nding the right bus," as he puts it. This approach aims to
ensure that the departure is seen as an opportunity for the
employee to nd a position that better ts their skills and
aspirations.

Ethical Considerations and MSI’s Core Values
Maintaining Integrity and Respect
Manu is acutely aware of the impact of termination on an

The Peter Principle and Organizational Fit
Understanding Limits of Competency
MSI acknowledges the Peter Principle, which suggests
that employees often rise to their level of incompetence.
This principle is discussed openly, helping leaders and
employees understand that not every promotion or role is
suitable for every individual. This awareness helps frame
terminations not as personal failures but as mismatches
between role requirements and individual capabilities.

Conclusion: Compassionate Departures
In conclusion, while terminations are never easy, MSI
handles them with a focus on what is best for both the
individual and the company. By ensuring that terminations
are handled respectfully, ethically, and in alignment with
corporate values, MSI maintains its reputation as a fair and
principled employer. The goal is always to enable former
employees to transition smoothly to opportunities that
align better with their skills and career goals, thereby
turning a challenging situation into a constructive pathway
for future success.

Raj & Rup
This chapter explores the leadership journey of Raj and Rup Shah
at MSI. Growing up immersed in the family business, they gained
external experience in investment banking before o cially joining
MSI in 2003. Their leadership has been instrumental in MSI’s
exponential growth in revenue, operations, and workforce.
Succession planning and mentoring by their father, Manu, have
ensured a smooth transition while maintaining MSI’s core values of
quality and integrity.

Identifying and Nurturing Future Leaders

Challenges in Grooming the Next Generation
Grooming the next generation of leaders is not without

mission.
Moreover, the transition of leadership can sometimes
create internal con icts. "It's natural for there to be some

with transparency and mutual respect."

This chapter outlines MSI’s commitment to maintaining nancial

The company follows the principle of “Follow the Cash” to monitor
transactions and detect any irregularities. MSI takes swift action
when misconduct is suspected, including thorough investigations
and legal proceedings if necessary. To prevent issues, MSI
continually strengthens its internal controls with regular audits,
employee training, and transparent nancial reporting systems.
This vigilant approach ensures MSI upholds its ethical standards
and fosters a culture of accountability, protecting its assets and
reputation.

n the journey of transforming MSI from a modest

the development of cohesive and e ective teams has been
