creating a competitive yet supportive atmosphere, where
the notion of losing is not entertained, and each team
member is geared towards a collective victory. This chapter
delves into the strategic facets of fostering a robust team
culture that drives MSI’s continual growth and success.

The Game of Growth - Building a Competitive
Team Spirit
Manu believes in infusing elements of gami cation into
team activities, enhancing engagement and motivation
across the board. He views the business landscape as a
competitive game where having a clear set of goals and
tasks is crucial for victory. Each team member is assigned
speci c roles with clear accountability, embodied in the
concept of the Directly Responsible Individual (DRI). This
structure ensures that everyone knows their
responsibilities and how they contribute to the team's
objectives.

Strategic Opposition - Utilizing Competition to
Foster Unity
One of Manu’s unique strategies in team building is the
utilization of a real or imagined adversary. By de ning a
common enemy—sometimes a competing company,
sometimes a challenging project, and at times, even time

fi

fi

ff

fi

408

itself—Manu creates a unifying force within the team. The
presence of an "enemy" minimizes internal con icts and
focuses the team on the external challenge, driving them to
pool their strengths and work cooperatively.

Supporting the Underdog - Strengthening the
Weakest Link
A key tenet of Manu’s team-building philosophy is that "a
team is only as strong as its weakest member." To this end,
considerable resources and time are dedicated to training
and uplifting the team's less skilled members. This not only
enhances the overall competency of the team but also
fosters a culture of support and empathy, reinforcing the
idea that everyone’s growth contributes to the team’s
success.

Prioritization and Focus - Choosing Battles
Wisely
Manu advises that while it’s important to have high
aspirations, recognizing the team's limits is essential for
e ective strate y execution. Not all goals can be pursued
simultaneously without diluting the team's e orts. He
advocates for selecting strategic goals that align with the
company’s core mission while postponing or delegating
others. This selective focus ensures that the team does not
become overwhelmed and can achieve signi cant victories
one step at a time.

ff

fi

g

Recognition and Renewal - Celebrating Success

After every project or challenge, Manu makes it a point to
celebrate the team's achievements, regardless of the
outcome. He emphasizes the importance of acknowledging
everyone’s e orts and then resetting the objectives for the
next challenge. This routine of recognition followed by
renewal keeps the team motivated and focused on
continuous improvement.

Building Teams of Teams - Expanding the
Team Building Model
As MSI grows, the concept of a 'team of teams' becomes
integral. This approach involves creating multiple
interlinked teams that operate with a degree of autonomy
yet are aligned towards the company’s overarching goals.
This structure supports scalability and adaptability,
enabling MSI to manage its expanding operations more
e ectively.

Conclusion: A Culture of Positive Team
Dynamics
These strategies have created a strong team culture at
MSI, de ned by positivity, mutual support, and a shared
commitment to the company's goals. Manu’s approach to

and fostered a dynamic workplace where every team
member can thrive.

Embracing Digital Innovation in Porcelain

requirements. His focus was on enhancing MSI’s import

visionary leadership and a sharp focus on industry trends

Then came 2010, a pivotal year for the industry. Quartz
surfaces, which replicated the look of granite but with a
man-made touch, began to rise in popularity. These slabs,
available in 3CM or 2CM thickness, started to address many
of the issues associated with natural stone. Made up of 92%
natural quartz and 8% resin, these factory-produced slabs
could mimic the appearance of granite or marble in just 48
hours—an extraordinary feat considering that mother
nature takes 30 million years to achieve the same result.
MSI saw the potential in this emerging market and
quickly entered the fray. They didn’t just stop at
introducing quartz slabs; they went a step further by
creating marble-like surfaces with superior properties,
particularly suited for kitchen use. This innovation became
a game changer. By 2020, MSI had captured over 15% of the
market, with an even larger share in the highly sought-after
white Carrara and Calacatta looks. MSI dominated this
segment from coast to coast in the USA, cementing its place
as a leader in the quartz surface market.

This chapter explores the Shah family’s re ections on business

chapter emphasizes the lessons learned from missteps with
suppliers, product lines, and di cult family relationships. Manu

decision-making, surrounding oneself with positive in uences, and
learning from past mistakes.

The Struggle Against Negativity
For Manu, one of the most challenging aspects of his

seem to revel in negativity, constantly complaining or
nding fault. Over time, he realized the toll such negativity
takes, not just on morale, but on the very fabric of

progress. Surrounding himself with positive, constructive

Family Emotions
Emotional involvement within the family or extended

rational, or logical answers when emotions are at play. One

Lessons Learned

These experiences have reinforced several key lessons:
• The importance of making informed, strategic
decisions to avoid unsustainable paths.
• The need to surround oneself with positive in uences
and minimize exposure to negativity.
• The understanding that family dynamics can be
complex and emotionally taxing, often lacking clear
solutions.
• Acquiring another business in 1992 94 and 1998 2001

429

with unimpressive results! Moving forward, MSI made

ones that were made had a very clear strategic value.
Through these re ections, Manu emphasizes the
importance of learning from regrets and using these
lessons to guide future actions, ensuring a more positive
and productive path forward.
Learning from regrets in business is a powerful tool for
improvement and growth. When we re ect on our regrets,
we can gain insights into where we went wrong and why.
This process allows us to identify the decisions and actions
that led to undesirable outcomes. By understanding our
past mistakes, we can make better decisions in the future,
avoid repeating the same errors, and improve our business
strategies.
Here are ways learning from regrets (“Failure is the Best
Coach”) can help improve business:
• Enhanced Decision-Making: Carefully considering what
we regret and why helps us to reverse course or make
better decisions moving forward.
• Cultivating a Learning Culture: Recognizing that failure
is inevitable in complex organizations, leaders can create
a culture where employees feel safe to admit or report
failures, leading to consistent reporting and deep analysis
of mistakes.
• Strategic Improvements: Regrets can signal that
improvement is possible. The key is to avoid obsessing

fl

fl

430

over them and instead extract lessons that can be applied
in future situations.
• Better Risk Management: Re ecting on past regrets can
improve our decision-making skills, especially in
assessing potential risks and bene ts before making
business decisions.
• Personal Growth: Regrets can be useful tools for
personal growth, helping us to understand our values
and priorities better, which can translate into more
aligned and e ective business practices.
In summary, while it’s natural to experience regret, it’s
how we use those feelings to inform our future actions that
can lead to signi cant improvements in our business
operations and outcomes. It’s about transforming regret
into a proactive force for positive change.
Regrets Are Inevitable. Start Learning From Them.
(hbr.org)

This chapter chronicles MSI’s evolution from a small home-based

marked the beginning of a new chapter, setting the stage

Catering to Changing American Home
Preferences
During this time, American homeowners began to shift
their preferences from carpeted oors, which were
associated with allergies, to harder surface materials. This
change in consumer taste opened new opportunities for
MSI. The company began importing marble from Spain,
granite tiles, and trendy multicolored slates from India,

in American homes declined from covering 82% of oors to

3.24
Leadership Lessons from
Chess
This chapter re ects on how Manu applied strategic lessons from
chess to business leadership. The game taught him the importance
of understanding competitors, being patient, protecting key assets,
and planning every move with a clear goal in mind. Manu’s strategic
thinking, adaptability, and long-term vision were shaped by these
principles, helping MSI navigate challenges and capitalize on
opportunities.

fl

462

n his teenage years,
Manu developed a
deep a nity for the game
of chess. Though barely
an above-average player,
he quickly realized that
chess was more than just
a game; it was a powerful
metaphor for leadership
and business strate y.
The lessons he learned at
the chessboard began to
in uence his decisions in real life, especially in business. As
MSI grew and ourished, Manu found himself relying on
these strategic insights, which had been ingrained in him
through the calculated moves of chess. Here are the key
lessons from chess that in uenced Manu’s business
leadership:
1. Understand Your Opponent's Forces
In chess, it's crucial to understand the position, potential
moves, and threats posed by your opponent's pieces. This
mirrors business, where competitors deploy resources,
such as purchasing power, inventory, and workforce to gain
an advantage. In leading MSI, Manu always took time to
analyze the competition's strengths and weaknesses—be it
their market position, attrition rate, or working hours—and
adjusted MSI’s strategies accordingly. Like a chess player,
he sought to stay ahead by anticipating the next move of
competitors and planning counterstrategies.

fl

g

fl

ffi

fl

463

2. Play a Defensive Game to Tire Out Your Opponent
Patience is essential in chess, especially when playing
defensively. The game can often become a waiting match,
where you outlast your opponent's attempts to break
through your defenses. Manu applied this principle in
business, particularly when entering competitive markets
or when resources were tight. The key was not to expect
quick miracles but to play the long game, allowing
competitors to tire themselves out while conserving MSI’s
strength for a decisive moment. This defensive posture
enabled MSI to thrive during economic downturns and
crises when other companies faltered.
3. Do Not Attack Unless You Are Sure to Win
Chess teaches that launching an attack prematurely can
leave you exposed, leading to disastrous consequences.
Manu learned that in business, attacking competitors headon without a thorough understanding of the consequences
can back re. Whether expanding into new markets or
launching new products, Manu ensured that MSI’s moves
were calculated, and that victory was almost certain. He
was careful not to open any " anks" without adequate
preparation, knowing that a poorly timed initiative could
invite competitors to strike at his vulnerabilities.
4. Protect Your Queen & All Your Best Assets
In chess, the queen is your most valuable piece, and
losing it often means losing the game. For Manu, the queen
represented MSI’s senior leaders and top talent—those
indispensable assets who were key to the company’s

fl

fi

464

success. He understood the importance of protecting these
assets at all costs. Senior leadership at MSI was nurtured,
appreciated, and supported because Manu knew that the
loss of a key leader could lead to a cascade of negative
e ects throughout the organization. Retaining top talent
became a hallmark of MSI’s leadership philosophy.
Manu took this principle even further by instituting a nolayo policy at MSI, a commitment that re ects his deep
belief in the intrinsic value of his workforce. Unlike many
companies that might reduce sta during challenging
times, MSI has never laid o any employee due to a lack of
work. This approach not only preserves the company's
strength and morale but also fosters a culture of loyalty and
mutual respect, reinforcing the idea that employees are the
true "queens" of the organization—indispensable to its
success.

5. Think Before Every Move with a Clear Goal in
Mind
Chess is a game of foresight, where each move must be
carefully considered in the context of a larger goal. Manu
approached business with the same mindset. Before
making major decisions, he always considered the broader

purpose. This clarity of vision kept MSI on a steady growth
trajectory for decades.

Expanding the Chess Metaphor: Leaders as

1. Knowing When to Use Each Piece: Leaders Are Like
Chess Pieces
In chess, every piece has a distinct role, and the success
of a player depends on knowing when and how to use each
piece e ectively. The queen, the most powerful piece on
the board, is your go-to for o ensive maneuvers, while
pawns, though often overlooked, can be used to open the
board and provide critical defense. Similarly, in business,
leaders must recognize the strengths and roles of their
team members, each of whom brings unique skills to the
table. Manu has always understood this concept, likening
di erent leadership roles in MSI to the various chess pieces.
• The Queen (Executive Leadership): Just as the

executive leadership at MSI has the most in uence.
Whether it's guiding the company toward new markets,

• The Rook (Operations Leadership): The rook moves

the same way, the operations leadership at MSI is

• The Bishop (Product or Market Development): The

unique ways. This is akin to the product or market
development teams at MSI, who must think creatively
and nd angles others may miss. Their job is to keep the
company competitive and ahead of industry trends.
• The Knight (Sales and Marketing): The knight moves
in an "L" shape, jumping over other pieces and executing
unconventional moves. This represents the sales and
marketing teams at MSI, who often have to think outside
the box, nd innovative ways to reach customers, and
sometimes make bold moves to capture market share.
• The Pawn (Entry-Level Leaders and Employees):
Though pawns are the least powerful pieces, they are
critical in setting up the board and paving the way for
more strategic moves. In MSI, the entry-level employees,
often seen as pawns, are vital for executing day-to-day
tasks. Their work is essential for larger, strategic
initiatives, and, given the right conditions, they can be
promoted, just as a pawn can be promoted to a queen.
Understanding how and when to use these di erent
"pieces" has been critical to Manu’s leadership. Like a chess
player, Manu evaluates each situation to determine which
leaders and employees are best suited to tackle speci c

fi

ff

fi

challenges. The goal is always to align their strengths with

2. Applying Lessons Even When You’re Not Playing
While some leaders may not be the best at playing chess,
they excel at applying its principles in real-world situations.
Chess teaches that every move must be calculated, with
consequences considered long before they happen. It’s not
about being the best player, but about learning the lessons
that chess imparts and knowing how to use those lessons to
lead e ectively. For Manu, chess was never just a game—it
was a blueprint for leadership.
Manu learned that in both chess and business, it's
essential to stay calm under pressure and think several
moves ahead. The ability to foresee potential outcomes and
respond accordingly is what separates a good leader from a
great one. Similarly, the willingness to sacri ce lesser assets
for the sake of a larger goal—while ensuring that core values
and key leaders (your "queen") are protected—is a lesson
that Manu has applied time and again throughout his
career.

operations and building trust across the supply chain, sales

Employees, who can openly communicate any

Transparency vs. Privacy/Con dentiality:
It's essential to emphasize that transparency, while a
vital principle for organizational growth, often comes into
tension with privacy and con dentiality. This complexity
can be especially challenging when considering sensitive
issues like employee privacy and con dential business
matters.

fi

fi

fi

ff

478

Transparency is highly valued in a business environment,
but it must be balanced with the privacy of employees. For
instance, an employee who is going through a personal
matter, such as a divorce or pregnancy, should have the
right to disclose that information only when they feel
ready. It's critical that companies respect these boundaries
and protect the personal lives of their employees.
Similarly, con dentiality in business—particularly
around sensitive information like pay structures, salary
raises, or bonus amounts—must take precedence. This
ensures that internal nancial information is handled
delicately and only shared when necessary to maintain
trust and professionalism within the company.
Ultimately, the guiding principle for managing these
complexities is the "Need to Know" basis. Leaders and
employees should only have access to information if it's
critical for decision-making or directly impacts their work.
This balance allows companies like MSI to maintain
transparency while safeguarding personal and con dential
information, ensuring the company operates with integrity
and respect for all.
By evaluating the need to know in every case, MSI can
continue to foster an open and transparent work culture
without compromising the privacy and con dentiality that
are equally crucial for long-term success.
It's also crucial to recognize that con dentiality and
privacy extend beyond the workplace and into our
personal lives. In today's digital age, personal privacy is
more important than ever, and it touches every aspect of

fi

fi

fi

fi

fi

479

our interactions, including with family members, friends,
and acquaintances.
Just as we carefully protect business information, we
must also respect the con dentiality of personal details.
For instance, something as simple as disclosing
someone’s mobile phone number or sharing details
about their personal life, like where they live, what car
they’re planning to buy, or even their health problems,
should never be done without explicit permission. Each
individual should have the autonomy to decide who has
access to their personal information, including mobile
devices, phone numbers, and even their thoughts on
sensitive matters like career changes or medical conditions.
In an era where personal boundaries are critical, the
principle of "Need to Know" should also apply to personal
life. Respecting the privacy and con dentiality of others in
their personal and professional lives re ects a deeper
understanding of mutual respect and integrity. It's
essential that these values be upheld both within and
outside the business environment to maintain trust and
harmony in relationships.

This chapter explores how the introduction of the IBM PC in 1981
revolutionized MSI’s operations. Early adoption of this technolo y

like electronic spreadsheets, the internet, and email, these tools

solidifying MSI’s competitive edge in the industry.

hen IBM introduced the personal computer to

Within a few months of its debut in

Kishor Panchani, Senior Vice President - Service : IT

had done more than a decade prior. This was a

520

Historical Context: The Rise of Mobile Technolo y in Business
The use of mobile phones in business began as a luxury, only
a ordable and practical for select industries. Early mobile phones,
like the one used at MSI in 1990, were primarily for emergency
communication. However, by the mid-1990s, as companies like
Nokia and Motorola introduced portable, more a ordable phones,
the business world began to recognize the untapped potential of
mobile communication. These innovations laid the foundation for
what would become an essential part of global commerce. By the
2000s, 3G networks revolutionized how data was transferred over
mobile devices, and the introduction of smartphones by Apple in
2007 fundamentally changed how businesses operated, including
MSI. The leap from a simple communication device to a
multifunctional business tool marked the beginning of a new era in
enterprise technolo y.
Technological Context: From Limited to Limitless
Communication
The early mobile devices had severe limitations in terms of
battery life, call time, and functionality. But as technolo y
advanced, mobile phones evolved into powerful business tools. 2G
networks allowed for the advent of text messaging and limited data
capabilities, which were enhanced by the 3G networks that followed.
The real breakthrough came with smartphones, particularly the
iPhone, which combined communication with computing power.
This convergence of technolo y allowed businesses to access a suite
of applications, ranging from email to full-scale enterprise resource
planning (ERP) systems, all from the convenience of a mobile device.
At MSI, this meant more agile decision-making, enhanced customer
service, and improved operational e ciency, making mobile
technolo y an integral part of the company's strate y.

Conclusion
As of 2024, there are over 7.21 billion smartphones in use
worldwide, which constitutes around 60.42% of the global
population owning a smartphone. This is a signi cant
increase from earlier years, showing how essential
smartphones have become for personal and business use.
Furthermore, including both smart and feature phones, the
total number of mobile phone connections globally has
surpassed 8.31 billion. This number re ects the rapid
growth and penetration of mobile technolo y, driven by
the increasing demand for connectivity, especially with the
rise of 3G and 4G networks, and now the proliferation of 5G
networks.

and postage costs while enhancing communication speed. Email
revolutionized interactions with customers, suppliers, and internal
teams, becoming a strategic tool for improving operational

n the early 1990s, the

The Email Revolution: Instant Communication
and Action
With the introduction of email, MSI saw an immediate
improvement in the speed and e ciency of
communication. Unlike traditional mail, which could take
days or even weeks to reach its recipient, emails allowed
for instant delivery and immediate action. This instant
communication was particularly bene cial in:
• Customer Interactions: Prompt responses to customer
inquiries and faster resolution of issues plus Digital
invoices, Statements, Orders and payments receipts.

ffi

Desk top deposits and ECH or EFT (Electronic Fund

Customs for overseas containers.
• Employee Communication: Streamlined internal
communication, and direct deposit of checks to their
bank accounts enabling quicker decision or action
making processes.
• Sales and Marketing: Rapid dissemination of marketing
materials and promotional o ers including Digital
Catalogs.
Signi cant Cost Reductions
One of the most notable impacts of adopting email
communication was the signi cant reduction in costs
associated with printing and postage. Manu estimated that
a single printed paper, costing about 4 cents, incurred
additional expenses of 2 cents for printing and nearly 40
cents in labor for handling, ling, and mailing. By moving
to email, MSI was able to drastically cut these costs. Some
key areas where cost savings were realized include:
• Reduction in Postage Expenses: In 1997, MSI’s postage
expenses were $9,000. Despite the company's
exponential growth over the next 25 years, these
expenses remained nearly the same, thanks to the switch
to electronic communication.

• Savings on Printing and Paper: The transition to email

tasks.
Developing Clear Guidelines and Follow-Through

timely responses.

archived and easily retrievable, enhancing e ciency in
information management.
• Security Protocols: Implementing measures to protect
Sensitive information and prevent data breaches.
• Training Programs: Conducting training sessions to
familiarize employees with the new system and its
bene ts.

Showroom Concept
This chapter explains how MSI revolutionized the retail industry
in 1996 by introducing warehouse showrooms, blending the
functionality of a retail showroom with the convenience of a
warehouse. This model allowed customers to directly interact with
products in a practical and cost-e ective setting, enhancing the
overall shopping experience. Today, MSI’s network of warehouse
showrooms continues to o er a unique, e cient approach to retail
while maintaining customer satisfaction and operational e ciency.

ffi

ffi

ff

ff

n 1996, MSI revolutionized the retail industry by
introducing the concept of a showroom attached to a
warehouse. Our rst showroom, spanning 3000 sq feet,
was located within one of our warehouses at Allec Street,
Anaheim, and was the rst of its kind in the industry. The
idea behind the warehouse showroom was to provide a

unique customer experience by allowing customers to see,
touch, and feel our products before making a purchase. At
MSI, we have always believed in nding innovative
solutions to meet the needs of our customers, and the
warehouse showroom concept was no exception.
Being primarily a part of the wholesale business, we
understood the challenges that came with it. Most of our
competitors had warehouses attached to retail locations on

fi

fi

fi

541

main streets, which, although consumer-friendly, came
with a high cost of rent. We made a thoughtful decision to
locate our warehouses on side streets, which allowed us to
save on rent costs. However, we knew that to stand out and
attract customers; we needed to think of something unique.
That’s where the idea of attaching showrooms to our
warehouses came into play. By doing so, we were able to
o er customers the best of both worlds: the convenience of
browsing and purchasing products at the warehouse and
the experience of a traditional retail showroom.
The bene ts of having warehouse showrooms were
multiple. The larger space allowed us to showcase a greater
variety of products, giving customers a wider range of
options to choose from. Additionally, our contractors and
fabricators could see and feel the products in person,
which helped them make more informed decisions
regarding their own projects. But perhaps most
importantly, our entire space was designed with our
customers in mind. From the layout to the lighting, every
aspect was crafted to create a comfortable and enjoyable
shopping experience. By putting our customers rst, we
created a unique and successful retail experience in a
wholesale environment that set us apart from our
competitors.
Our approach to retail is unique, as we focus on curating
an experience for the customers of our customers. While
we stopped dealing directly with homeowners in 2010, we

Pioneering Productivity:
Strategic Leap with Dual
Screens
This chapter explains how MSI's universal adoption of dualscreen setups in 2002 boosted productivity by 7 10%, enhancing
multitasking, reducing errors, and improving communication
across its o ces. By implementing this innovative practice, MSI
signi cantly increased operational e ciency, positioning itself as a
leader in workplace innovation and setting a new standard for
productivity in the industry.

-

ffi

ffi

fi

549

t MSI, we believe in pushing the boundaries of
innovation to fuel our growth and enhance our
operational e ciency. A testament to this core philosophy
is our historic decision in 2002 to adopt dual-screen setups
across the entire company—a pioneering move that not only
signi cantly boosted our productivity but also set industry
standards.

A Historic Decision
In the early 2000s, dual monitors were more of a novelty
than a necessity in corporate environments. Recognizing
the potential to drastically improve employee e ciency,
Manu made a strategic decision to equip every workstation
with dual screens. This bold move made us the rst in the
industry to adopt such a setup universally, marking a
signi cant milestone in our journey of innovation.

ffi

fi

ffi

fi

fi

550

The results were immediate and profound. By integrating
dual screens, we saw a productivity increase of 7 10%
across the company. This enhancement in productivity has
been a key factor in our ability to expand and scale our
operations more e ectively than ever before without
having to proportionately increase our team sizes.

The Bene ts of Dual Screens:
• Increased Productivity: The additional screen real
estate allows us to handle multiple tasks simultaneously
with ease, signi cantly cutting down on the time
required to toggle between applications and increasing
the throughput of work processed.
• Enhanced Multitasking: Dual screens facilitate an
environment where critical information can be displayed
continuously, aiding in faster decision-making and more
uid work ows—essential components in elds that
demand high levels of accuracy and speed.
• Optimized Workspace: A dual-monitor setup helps
maintain a cleaner and more organized digital
workspace. This organization is crucial for reducing
cognitive load and focusing more on the tasks at hand.
• Reduced Errors and Improved Accuracy: With data
and applications spread across two monitors, we are less
likely to make errors due to misreading information or
losing track of tasks. This setup particularly bene ts roles

that involve extensive data entry, coding, or

nancial

• Enhanced Communication and Collaboration:

• Increased Employee Satisfaction and Retention:
Team members equipped with dual monitors often
experience less stress and frustration, leading to higher

• Better Customer Service: Dual screens can greatly

Challenges and Considerations
Despite the clear advantages, adopting dual monitors was
not without its challenges. It required a signi cant upfront
investment and a shift in IT infrastructure. At the time of
initial adoption, when MSI Services team went to procure
dual monitors in the Indian market, most PC vendors
hadn’t even heard of the concept and it took some time to
procure the appropriate hardware. However, the strategic
foresight to implement this change has paid dividends, far
outweighing the initial setup costs and hiccups.

3.37
Account Receivable - SCARE
Costs - An Innovative Tool
This chapter discusses the introduction of MSI's SCARE Costs
system, which tracks and analyzes transactional expenses such as
shipping, credit card fees, and restocking. By providing
transparency and insights into the true pro tability of each
transaction, the system empowers employees to make informed,
entrepreneurial decisions, fostering a more strategic and e cient
business environment.

ffi

fi

574

n 2009, MSI introduced a groundbreaking concept
known as SCARE Costs to promote entrepreneurship
and transparency, which lies at the heart of our business
philosophy. SCARE Costs revolutionized our accounts
receivable practices, o ering a fresh approach to
understanding the true cost of doing business. This
innovative idea allows MSI to record and analyze the costs
associated with every transaction in a way that adds value
and insight.

SCARE is an acronym derived from:
• Shipping Expenses – freight cost of the transaction.
• Credit Card Charges – Expenses incurred by MSI
when customers pay by credit cards
• A/R expenses – Finance cost associated with the time
taken by the customer to pay their open balance

ff

575

• Restocking Charges – The cost of processing returned
material and restocking it.
• Other Expenses – All other expenses, such as
merchandising, rebates, holding costs, etc.
This system allows MSI to constantly ne-tune our
business practices, moving at the speed of imagination to
deliver the most value to our trading partners. Through
SCARE Costs, we have created a unique approach that
optimizes resource usage, builds value, and di erentiates
us in the marketplace. By translating transactional data into
actionable business insights, SCARE Costs bring
transparency at multiple levels.

The Power of Transparency
SCARE Costs provide us with a new lens through which
to view our business transactions. It presents a clear
picture of customer behavior, enabling us to adjust our
sales strategies to better align with each customer’s
business model. This visibility allows us to track, compare,
and re ne our business practices in real time. The true
power of SCARE Costs lies in its ability to bring granular
details to the forefront, ensuring that every transaction is
measured and understood in terms of its actual
pro tability.

The Decisions That Matter
SCARE Costs empower our team to make informed
decisions. How you conduct business with your customers,

ff

fi

fi

fi

576

based on their transactional behavior, is up to you. SCARE
simply provides the tools to visualize and understand that
behavior.
• Comparative Analysis: SCARE Costs enable easy
comparisons between customers, salespeople, or team
performances, ensuring that we can measure success on
a level playing eld. This apples-to-apples comparison
helps us understand each transaction’s true pro tability
and drives behavior change across our organization.
• Entrepreneurial Empowerment: By providing
salespeople with the freedom to tailor transactions
according to their customers' needs, SCARE promotes
entrepreneurial thinking. The saying "With freedom
comes responsibility" rings true here. SCARE helps us
determine not only who we should do business with but
also who we might choose to avoid.

fi

fi

577

• Accurate Cost Allocation: SCARE Costs give us the
ability to accurately allocate and measure the expenses
involved in generating revenue, beyond just the cost of
goods sold (COGS). This comprehensive understanding of
transaction costs allows us to make more informed
decisions that drive pro tability.
As a result, MSI has cultivated a culture that emphasizes
optimal resource utilization, cost reduction, and
productivity enhancement. SCARE Costs also help ensure
accountability across the board, as everyone is
empowered to make entrepreneurial decisions within
their sphere of in uence.
Kush Shah (Vice President: Business Innovation) sums
it up: "SCARE Costs allow each individual to make
entrepreneurial decisions in their spheres of in uence."

Keeping it Simple
Imagine you own a ower shop. Every day, you buy
1,000 fresh roses with the goal of selling them for a pro t.
But beyond the cost of the roses themselves, you’ve
invested in supplies like containers, oral tape, wrapping
paper, and more—adding $0.50 to the cost of each rose. You
also pay for shipping, credit card fees, and other expenses.
If you don’t account for all these costs when setting your
prices, you could end up with lower pro ts or even a loss.
Many businesses overlook these granular costs, failing to
provide their sales teams with the visibility needed to make
informed decisions. SCARE Costs are one of MSI’s key
innovations, enabled by technolo y, to track and allocate

fi

fl

fi

fl

g

fi

fl

fl

578

every expense related to each transaction. It’s crucial to
understand that these are not additional charges—they’re
the inherent costs of doing business, now made visible.
SCARE Costs help us maintain pro t margins across all
customers, regardless of their purchasing behavior.

Clarifying the Confusion
Some expenses are di cult to assign accurately, which is
why many companies label them as overhead costs and
ignore them at the transactional level. MSI, however, has
leveraged technolo y to e ectively calculate and allocate
these expenses as accurately as possible.

Our leaders continuously re ne the accuracy of these
allocations and the work ows related to SCARE Costs to
ensure fairness and transparency. For example, in August
2021, MSI began charging a transaction fee for all payments
made by credit card. Initially, there were concerns that
these fees were being allocated without considering their

fi

fi

ff

fl

ffi

g

579

It’s important to note that SCARE Costs can have both
positive and negative e ects on pro t margins. For
instance, when invoices are paid within 45 days, the
nance cost decreases, thus increasing the margin.
Conversely, invoices paid after 45 days incur higher nance
costs, reducing the margin.

The Bottom Line
SCARE Costs have unlocked a new level of business
pro ciency at MSI, bene ting all stakeholders by driving
behavior change. This innovative approach maximizes
resource utilization, adapts to diverse business patterns,
and ensures pro tability across all transactions. When fully
understood, SCARE is a powerful tool. As Aakash Mehta
(Senior Sales Representative: CAOR) aptly put it, "We
shouldn’t call it SCARE; we should call it CARES!"

fi

fi

ff

fi

fi

fi

fi

580
fl

fi

impact on overall pro tability. In response, the process was
adjusted to charge customers a fee when paying by credit
card, and the Credit Card SCARE Costs were recalculated to
re ect the net expenses after fee collections.

is a strategic initiative to maximize storage space. This

Land Reclamation: Converting unused areas into valuable
storage space to increase capacity.
Environmental Considerations: Ensuring that land
reclamation projects are sustainable and environmentally
friendly.
Cost E ciency: Reducing the need for new land
acquisition by optimizing existing resources.

Many of MSI facilities have or had railroad access from
1950’s. None of them serve MSI by Rail anymore. Those
were bulk supply before the days of Containers. All that
land is owned by properties railroad passing thru. Railroad
has right of way from each property. Rail may be live few
times a week or dead- No longer being use. At our HQ in

Orange CA as per attached pdf one edge had 1650 Feet long
and other side 500 feet edge. Railroad now are never wider
than 10.5 feet – so total clear path needed can be 20 feet. So
Manu with Kishor at MSI decided to reclaim the space over
20’ wide. Total reclaimed on one side was 26000 sq feet
and on another side 2000 sqft. Several other MSI facilities –
old rails were converted in to usable space by adding
concrete over it. Every Sq feet matters!

3.39
Detailed Employee
Turnover Analysis - A Case
Study of MSI Started in 2014
This chapter discusses MSI’s Employee Turnover Analysis,
introduced in 2014 to track and understand retention patterns. By
analyzing turnover through lters like location, team, and pay
structure, MSI identi es areas for improvement and implements
strategies such as enhanced onboarding, management training, and
competitive compensation, ultimately boosting employee retention
and satisfaction.

fi

590

Innovative Turnover Analysis at MSI, Why &
How?
Available to all Supervisors down the ladder view from
Senior VP to Operations Directors; always updated and can
be used instantaneously; overview, trend and details as
needed; Scalable, Measurable and transparent; overall goal
to be Annualized Attrition rate over 10% but under 20%;
Corrective steps easy to see and action can start.

Objective
This innovation aims to analyze employee turnover using
various lters to identify patterns, troubled areas, and
suggest corrective actions for MSI.

ff

fi

fi

onitoring
employee
turnover is crucial for
maintaining the
organizational health
of MSI. High turnover
rates can indicate
underlying issues
within the company, a ecting productivity, morale, and
nancial stability. Understanding turnover patterns helps
MSI implement strategies to retain talent and foster a
positive work environment.

Data Collection
Data is collected from MSI’s human resources
information system (HRIS), exit interviews, and employee
surveys. The dataset includes information on employee
demographics, employment history, and reasons for
leaving.

Filters Used
By Country, Employee Level, Team Type, Pay State, Pay
Type, Hiring Date, Time Period, reporting to (Supervisor),
Candidate Sourced From, Team, Work Location,
Designation.

Analysis & Turnover Trends
Here’s an example of how this works:
• Last 4 Months: To analyze recent turnover to identify
immediate issues.
• Last 4 Quarters: To provide a quarterly overview to
detect seasonal patterns.
• Last 2 Months Over Previous Year Same Two Months:
To compare recent turnover with the same period in the
previous year.
• Last Two Quarters vs. Last Two Years Same Quarter:
To analyze quarterly turnover over two years to observe
long-term trends.
592

• Last 4 Years: To examine long-term turnover patterns
to identify persistent issues.
• By Country: To identify turnover rates by country,
revealing potential cultural or economic factors a ecting
employee retention.
• By Employee Level: To analyze turnover among
di erent levels (entry, mid, senior) to understand where
attrition is highest.
• By Team Type: To compare turnover rates between
di erent team types (sales, technical, administrative).
• By Pay State and Pay Type: To examine how
compensation structure (salary, hourly) in uences
turnover.
• By Hiring Date: To look at turnover based on hiring
periods to identify trends in employee longevity.
• By Reporting to (Supervisor): To assess the impact of
direct supervisors on employee retention.
• By Candidate Sourced From: To evaluate turnover
based on recruitment sources (internal, referrals,
agencies).
• By Team: To analyze turnover within speci c teams to
pinpoint high-risk groups.
• By Work Location: To compare turnover rates across
di erent work locations (headquarters, branches).

Findings & Key Insights:
Certain countries or locations have a higher turnover,
which suggests potential cultural or regional economic
issues.
Another interesting observation is that senior-level
employees exhibit lower turnover rates compared to entrylevel.
Sales teams show higher turnover, indicating potential
job stress or dissatisfaction -– Some of them may be in the
wrong bus!
Hourly employees have higher turnover rates than
salaried employees. Often times, we nd out through deep
analysis that this could be due to the lack of proper training
or wrong expectations at the time of hiring. MSI never lays
o employees for the lack of work and gives upto 25%
overtime for most blue-collar workers. Almost everyone
gets some yearly bonus at MSI.
Employees hired during economic booms tend to leave
sooner. Many of MSI’s branches/ warehouses are located
next to Amazon or Target or other big box companies. This
is an advantage but name recognition can be a problem
when compared to national brands.

3.40
QR Codes: Streamlining MSI
Business with Smart
Technolo y
This chapter highlights MSI’s integration of QR codes, starting in
2014, to enhance e ciency across various aspects of the business.
QR codes are used in inventory tracking, order processing, product
samples, and even business cards, streamlining operations and
reducing errors. This technological innovation has improved both
internal work ows and customer engagement, reinforcing MSI’s
commitment to operational excellence.

g

ffi

fl

600

he COVID 19 pandemic catapulted the Quick
Response Codes (QR codes) into widespread
commercial use. From restaurants to retailers, everyone
started using QR Codes for providing menus to coupons to
be consumed contact-free. Long before the world at large
harnessed the power and versatility of the QR codes, MSI
was already incorporating them in various business and
marketing use cases. We were early adopters of QR codes to

streamline various processes, improve e ciency, and
increase customer engagement and experience. Today
everywhere you look in an MSI campus, you will see QR
codes: from LOT labels to door access and from business
cards to printed catalogs and ads.
MSI used QR codes in various ways since 2014, such as
inventory tracking, order processing, customer service,
marketing, and advertising.

ffi

-

601

MSI rst started using QR
Codes on our business
cards. Not only did they
make our business cards
more interactive, but they
also provided a convenient
and innovative way to pack a
lot of contact information in a small space. QR codes on our
business cards helped increase our visibility and made it
easier for our team members to digitally share their contact
information and reduce the use of paper cards. Paper cards
often get tossed around and possibly lost. With the QR
codes, we got directly into our customer’s smartphones
with a click of a button.
Incorporating QR codes into our sample products was a
revolutionary step taken by MSI, and we are proud to have
been the rst in the industry to do so. Our entire company
travels with the customer with QR codes on our samples.
By simply scanning the code, they can access complete and
up-to-date information they need about the product,
creating a lasting impression and solidifying the connection
with our brand. Not only does this bene t our customers,
but it also streamlines the sample order ful llment and
replenishment process for our team, making it easier for
them to track and manage the samples.

fi

fi

fi

fi

602

QR Codes for
QR Codes to Identify
Showroom Displays
LOTs
QR Codes for
Identifying Warehouse
Bulk Locations

QR codes are also a par t of our warehouse
management system (WMS) to streamline and improve
our ful llment processes. Some uses of QR codes in MSI
WMS include:
• Tracking inventory: QR codes representing the Lot
number and pallet numbers are attached to inventory
and scanned at various points in the warehouse to track
its location and movements.
• Picking and packing: QR codes guide our workers by
ensuring that the correct items are selected and packed
for each order.

fi

603

• Receiving: QR codes verify that the correct items have
been received and stored in the correct location.
• Shipping: QR codes are being used to identify pallets
and orders within a shipment.
Implementing QR code tracking in our WMS has
signi cantly enhanced e ciency and accuracy by tracking
the location and movements of each item, improving
inventory management, and reducing errors. We scan QR
codes approximately half a million times a month in the
warehouse. 55% of these scans are for inventory, 30% for
picking, 10% for shipping, and 5% for other activities.
Since implementing mandatory QR code scanning for
picking activities in the hubs, we’ve seen a 50% drop in
wrong material shipment errors. Additionally, QR
scanning on outbound shipping helps reduce left-o pallets
and ensures accurate tracking.
One of the greatest bene ts of QR technolo y is that it is
extremely fault tolerant and can easily recognize bent or
partially blocked codes. For example, you could run a
sharpie through a 2″x2″ QR code, and it is still likely to
recognize it. QR codes can take up to 30% structural
damage and still be recognizable. This makes them
perfectly suited for the unforgiving environment of a
warehouse.
We continue to look for opportunities to automate the
thinking side of warehouse tasks – i.e. by transitioning typing
to scanning and leverage AI/Big Data to predict potential
inventory related errors. – Matthew Zucker, Team Leader :
Operations Team, MSI, Orange, California.

ff

g

fi

ffi

fi

604

Using randomized QR codes for clocking in and out of
our warehouse has greatly improved the speed and
precision of our timekeeping system. We can track when
employees start and end their shifts by attaching QR codes
and linking them to our timekeeping software. It eliminates
the need for traditional time clocks or manual record
keeping. Additionally, using QR codes for clocking in and
out has allowed us to ensure the validity of the time clock
entries.
Using QR codes for accessing doors has greatly
improved security and convenience in our workplace. By
attaching QR codes to our o ce and warehouse building
doors, employees can use their smart devices to scan and
open the doors they are authorized for. This system also
allows us to grant or revoke employee access as and when
needed quickly. Additionally, using QR codes for door
access has allowed us to track who enters and exits
di erent parts of our buildings.
The amazing bene ts of using these tiny QR codes don’t
stop here. They can bridge physical and digital shopping
experiences, allowing users to access information easily.
Typing a long URL to visit websites, especially for di erent
products, can be cumbersome and discouraging to our
customers when they are browsing our showrooms or
catalogs. QR codes simplify that process and provide
instant grati cation for customers seeking more
information about the product, creating a pull e ect. Some
potential bene ts of using QR codes on catalogs include the
following:

ff

ff

ffi

fi

fi

fi

ff

605

• Enhanced accessibility: QR codes are easily scanned
with a smartphone or other smart device, allowing our
customers to quickly access information about our
products or services without needing a computer.
• Improved e ciency: QR codes streamline the
ordering process, allowing customers to easily place
orders or request more information without manually
entering product or contact details.
• Enhanced engagement: QR codes allow customers to
access interactive and engaging content, such as
visualizers, videos, demonstrations, or social media
pro les, which helps them learn more about our
products and connect with us in a more meaningful way.
We have seen great success with nearly 100,000 clicks
from showrooms, samples, and advertising QR codes
from January 2022 to January 2023.
QR codes also leverage cutting-edge technologies such as
drones and self-service lockers. By using QR codes to
interact with these technologies, we are able to streamline
processes and improve e ciency and accuracy. For
example, we are using drones to scan the QR codes to
check what inventory is sitting in which rack location.
MSI has always prided itself in being able to leverage
“High-Tech in a traditionally Low-Tech industry” to
optimize a commodity business and gain a competitive
advantage. Using QR codes is one such example. We
continue to leverage the power of QR codes in newer use
cases.

transformed the last-mile delivery process for non-slab pallet
shipments. Initially servicing a 90-mile radius, MSI Express now
completes over 90% of non-slab deliveries within a day, using
advanced logistics technolo y and a private eet. Equipped with
real-time tracking and route optimization software, MSI Express
enhances customer satisfaction by o ering greater control,
exibility, reliability, and cost e ciency in delivery operations.

Bene ts of MSI Delivery
MSI's private eet o ers numerous advantages over thirdparty trucking services, such as greater control, exibility,
and reliability. By utilizing its eet, MSI ensures that the
entire supply chain is managed in-house, from loading at
the warehouse to delivery at the customer's doorstep. This
minimizes handling and reduces the risk of product
damage. Additionally, MSI drivers are equipped with the
tools and training necessary to handle materials e ciently,
including Mo ett forklifts attached to the back of delivery
trucks.
MSI uses third-party FTL carriers for heavy-weight
shipments that demand an entire truck. For smaller-weight

With MSI Delivery we take the reins, and have full control

from start to nish. MSI Delivery o ers several advantages,

Bene ts of MSI Delivery
•
Greater Control: With MSI Delivery, the shipment
only gets handled when it gets loaded onto the truck and
when it reaches its destination. This minimizes the risk of
damage and helps reduce packaging costs.
•
Last Mile Delivery: The last mile refers to the
crucial movement of goods from the company’s
distribution center to the customer’s home or job site. It is
widely recognized as the most vital stage of the ful llment
and delivery process. It is important to get the products to
the customer’s doorstep in a timely, e cient, and coste ective manner to ensure customer satisfaction and
overall success for both the customer and the shipper. MSI
Delivery proudly o ers last-mile delivery within a 1-day
timeframe.

•

Streamlining Delivery with Descartes
To optimize operations and enhance delivery e ciency,
MSI uses Descartes Route Planner, an advanced routeplanning software that revolutionizes the way we optimize

geographical coverage. By optimizing the routing process,
we maximize e ciency, reduce travel time, and minimize
unnecessary mileage.
The result? Faster deliveries, reduced fuel consumption,
lower operational costs, and satis ed customers.
Additionally, the Descartes App allows drivers to capture
pictures of deliveries and gather customer signatures for
proof of delivery. By capturing a snapshot of the shipment
at the time of delivery, we can minimize claims and
maintain cleaner Accounts Receivable.

Historical Context
Geospatial analytics traces its origins back to the development of
geographic information systems (GIS) in the 1960s. Initially used in
government and defense sectors, the technolo y allowed for the
collection, visualization, and analysis of spatial data to improve
decision-making processes. With advancements in satellite
technolo y, digital mapping, and the rise of cloud computing, the
application of geospatial analytics expanded signi cantly in the late
20th and early 21st centuries. By the early 2000s, businesses began
incorporating geospatial tools to enhance supply chain

fi

g

ff

g

633

management, retail planning, real estate, and other operations.
Companies like Google revolutionized the accessibility of geospatial
data with the introduction of Google Maps, further democratizing
the use of location-based data.
MSI's adoption of geospatial analytics in business aligns with
this trend of leveraging spatial data to optimize resource allocation,
understand market demographics, and drive growth. This
approach has helped businesses, including MSI, gain a competitive
edge by allowing them to visualize and analyze data in ways that
were previously unattainable.

3.44
MSI Drones Take Flight
This chapter outlines MSI’s integration of drone technolo y to
enhance inventory management across its large warehouses. Faced
with challenges in manually tracking inventory and managing
replenishment in dynamic racking locations, MSI deployed
autonomous drones to streamline these processes. The drones
provide real-time data, improving accuracy and e ciency by
scanning inventory, capturing visual records, and triggering
replenishments. Despite some challenges, such as label
mismanagement and environmental factors, drones have
signi cantly improved inventory accuracy, reduced manual labor,
and contributed to MSI’s overall operational e ciency, including
outdoor monitoring. The chapter highlights MSI's commitment to
leveraging innovative technolo y for growth and e ciency.

g

ffi

ffi

ffi

g

fi

635

n MSI’s operations, units are stored in rack systems for
easy retrieval, with two types of bin locations: static
and dynamic. However, larger warehouses such as Orange,
California, Edison, New Jersey, the new Savannah, and the
upcoming port of Houston, have thousands of racking

locations. Ensuring accurate registration and tracking of
each pallet in the Wide Area Multilateration system has
become a challenge, particularly with dynamic locations.
As the order volume grows, proactively restocking depleted
and managing almost depleted rack inventory is
challenging, particularly a ecting the static locations used
for picking. To tackle these challenges, MSI is integrating
drone technolo y into its product lines to strategically
make use of the technolo y.
The current manual process of recording inventory
location and managing replenishment is resource-intensive

ff

g

g

636

and prone to human error. To make this process more
e ective and e cient, an automated solution is necessary.
Such a solution could provide accurate inventory counts,
identify empty racks for replenishment, and optimize the
use of warehouse resources. By automating these tasks, the
system could become the primary driver for inventory
management and replenishment in the racking storage. The
deployment of drone technolo y is proving to serve as the
foundation for such an automated solution, providing realtime inventory data and facilitating e cient replenishment.

What We Did
Developing and implementing a new technolo y solution
is a time-consuming and rigorous process that requires a lot
of thought and experimentation. Before deploying the
Corvus drones in our warehouses, we tried several other
methods that did not work for our speci c use case. About
seven years ago, Kush Shah (Vice President: Business
Innovation) led a project to build an in-house drone that fell
short of the goal of autonomous ight.
We also explored other technologies, including the use of
passive Radio-frequency identi cation tags on pallets,
forklift-mounted cameras, and pushcart-mounted iPads
with lidar, among others. Each of these methods presented
its own challenges that led to failure. Some had accuracy
issues, while others had physical obstructions and technical
limitations. We eventually focused our scope managing on
the single-deep racking, where we explored several
concepts before nally settling on the use of drones to
improve inventory tracking. Through this experience, we
learned that innovation requires patience, persistence, and

g

fi

ffi

fi

fl

g

fi

ffi

ff

637

an openness to try new ideas and methods without the fear
of failure.
Today, MSI has deployed autonomous drones that y
through the racks on predetermined schedules and paths.
These drones have two questions to answer: 1) What
inventory is sitting in which location? 2) How much of the
inventory is sitting in that location? (Is replenishment
required). The drones read the Lot QR codes on each pallet
and the item–location for each item on the rack. They also
visually estimate the occupancy of the rack and updates the
Wide Area Multilateration to trigger a replenishment if
required. Each time the drone passes the rack location, it
scans and stores a snapshot and a video for post- ight
audits. Drones have become the new and improved “eyes”
in MSI warehouses, o ering faster and more accurate
monitoring capabilities.
We want to integrate the drone technolo y with our
product lines and strategically leverage the technolo y to
drive e ciency, accuracy, and innovation. - Adaliza Diaz
Petrakakos (Operations Director, MSI, New Jersey).

Bene ts Of Using Drones To Track Inventory
• E ciency Boost: The adoption of drone technolo y
has dramatically increased the e ciency of our inventory
tracking process, reducing the time required compared
to manual scanning methods.
• Better Visibility & Auditing Capabilities: Drones
provide a snapshot of the pictures and videos from each
scan, which can be used for post-scan audits. This allows

fl

fl

g

g

g

ffi

ff

ffi

fi

ffi

638

• Autonomous Navigation: Drones are equipped with
3D mapping technolo y that enables them to
autonomously navigate the warehouse, including auto
docking. This reduces the need for human intervention,
helps keep MSI storage exible, and increases the speed
of scanning.
• Audit against the WAM System: The drone data is
easily mapped with MSI’s WAM data to verify and correct
discrepancies in the WAM system due to manual
processing.
• Data-sharing Capabilities: The drones have local
networking capabilities, allowing them to share data with
the MSI’s systems.
• Spotting Inaccuracies: The drone technolo y also
helps to identify discrepancies in tracking our Rack
inventory. For example in the Orange, California
warehouse, the drones nd about 30 discrepancies per
ight related to Lot Labels or WAM data. A drone scans
about 200 220 rack locations per ight.
The implementation of drone technolo y has had a
signi cant impact on our WAM accuracy. Our Edison, New
Jersey warehouse saw an improvement from a variance
of approximately 6% in November to 3% in January. This
improvement has allowed us to better ful ll customer
orders. Currently, we perform audits of our Edison, New
Jersey racking manually, but once all ve drones are in

Challenges
There are also some challenges with deploying drones.
To e ectively use drones, it is important that the inventory
is properly labeled and placed in an organized manner.
Often times the labels are missing, or damaged, or multiple
labels are placed on the same pallet leading to missed or
inaccurate readings.
Another challenge is the maintenance of drones. Our
warehouses, which have high dust environments, require
maintenance and the drones need to be constantly checked
and cleaned to ensure proper functioning. The dust and
debris can a ect the camera lenses of the drones.
Additionally, environment al conditions such as
temperature and lighting in the warehouse can also impact
the range and accuracy of the drones.
The use of drone technolo y in the warehouse is
therefore in uencing behavior to have better labeling and

g

g

fl

ff

640
ff

place, we can reduce the need for these manual audits. On
average, each ight in January saw the drones scanning
110 locations in Edison, New Jersey. With these timesaving and accuracy-enhancing capabilities, the dronebased inventory tracking solution is proving to be a
valuable asset for our warehouse operations.

proper care of the warehouse environment to overcome
some of these challenges.

Drones Outside The Warehouse
Our use of drones is not limited to just the rack space
inside our facilities, but also extends to the outdoors. In
Orange, California and Edison, New Jersey, we have
deployed GPS-enabled autonomous drones that literally
give a bird’s eye view of our facilities and help in
monitoring the tra c and parking situation. We also use
drones to monitor the condition of our exteriors. One of
the key bene ts of using drones for this purpose is the
ability to quickly and easily survey large areas without
having to physically inspect them. This has allowed us to
proactively identify and address potential issues quickly
and e ectively, saving us costly oversights.

Conclusion
Embracing technolo y is not easy. It is prone to multiple
failures before success can be achieved. However, if we stay
focused on the big picture as the drone does from a few
hundred feet above, we can derive value from it. New
technolo y and solutions not only help us do what we do
more accurately and e ciently but also in uence our
behavior to improve upon certain bad practices. We are
constantly looking for new ways to improve and make our
operations more e cient and the use of drones is just the
beginning.

4.1
Philanthropy and Strategic
Giving: A Legacy of Impact
Manu’s philanthropic journey, rooted in empathy and early acts
of kindness, evolved into strategic giving focused on job creation,
education, and healthcare. His initiatives, including vision care,
anemia prevention, and early childhood development, aim to make
a lasting impact on communities worldwide. Manu’s philosophy
emphasizes the transformative power of giving, aiming to inspire
others through his actions. His legacy is about creating positive
change and using innovation to enhance his philanthropic e orts.

ff

645

anu’s philanthropic journey began long before he
could de ne it. Growing up in a large family with
modest resources, he learned early on the importance of
kindness and empathy. "Education was the only way out of
poverty for my family," he often recalls, highlighting how
education became the cornerstone of his life’s purpose.
Even as a student, Manu felt compelled to share his limited
resources with those who had even less, whether it was
o ering his lunch or tutoring classmates. These small but
signi cant acts of kindness laid the foundation for a lifetime
of giving back.
Manu’s early experiences shaped his understanding of
philanthropy in its simplest form—helping others when you
can. "The word 'philanthropy' probably did not occur to me
until I was at least 50 years old," he admits. His generosity
was a natural extension of his empathy, a trait that would
later expand into more formal philanthropic e orts as his
business success grew.

From Acts of Kindness to Strategic
Philanthropy
As Manu’s career progressed, so did his ability to make a
broader impact. His informal acts of kindness evolved into
more structured and strategic initiatives as he realized that
his contributions could a ect entire communities. "I started
to see how my contributions could create a ripple e ect,
bene ting not just individuals but entire networks of
people," he says.

contributions scalable, ensuring that they had a lasting
impact. "I realized that philanthropy could be much more
than just helping one person at a time. It could help
reshape entire systems and communities."

Job Creation: A Powerful Form of
Philanthropy

For Manu, job creation is more than just a business goal;
it’s a form of philanthropy in itself. His belief in the power
of employment to transform lives is deeply ingrained.
"Creating jobs is in my rst nature," he emphasizes. Manu’s

thousands of jobs worldwide, and he views each of those

independence. People can take care of their families,

His philosophy aligns with that of other in uential

Fo r M a n u , p r o v i d i n g m e a n i n g f u l e m p l o y m e n t

4.3
Advancing Healthcare
through Innovation –
Manu’s Vision for Vision
Care
and

Prevention
This chapter discusses Manu Shah's commitment to advancing
healthcare through innovative, scalable solutions, particularly in
the areas of vision care and anemia prevention. Inspired by his
personal experiences with both issues, Manu focuses on using noninvasive technologies and local partnerships to improve access to
healthcare in underserved communities. His vision care initiative,
the Chashma Project, aims to provide eyeglasses to millions through
portable autorefractors and local production. His anemia initiative
leverages smartphone technolo y for early detection and
emphasizes education and nutrition to tackle root causes. Manu’s
healthcare approach is built on detection, treatment, and
community empowerment, striving to create sustainable solutions
for health equity.

g

663

anu’s personal journey into healthcare began in
his teenage years when he discovered that he had
vision problems and was anemic. Growing up in a large
family with limited access to healthcare, Manu wasn’t
aware that he needed glasses until a friend convinced him
to visit an optometrist. Similarly, he faced fatigue and
health issues in his youth due to anemia, a condition
caused by a lack of nutrition and awareness. These early
challenges sparked his commitment to ensuring that others
wouldn't su er from preventable health issues due to a lack
of access or education.
Today, Manu’s healthcare vision focuses on scalable, noninvasive technologies that can be deployed in resourceconstrained settings, making healthcare accessible to
millions. He believes in leveraging cutting- edge
technologies combined with strong local partnerships to
create sustainable solutions for two major health
challenges: vision care and anemia.

Vision Care: Solving a Global Crisis
At least 1 billion people
globally su er from vision
impairment, primarily due
to uncorrected refractive
errors. These impairments
a ect individuals' ability to
work, study, and lead
productive lives, resulting
in billions of dollars in lost
economic output. Despite

ff

ff

ff

664

the availability of a ordable eyeglasses, a lack of access to
vision care persists in low- and middle-income countries
(LMICs). This challenge is compounded by the shortage of
optometrists and the limited awareness of vision
impairment in underserved communities.
Manu’s vision care initiative, the Chashma Project, was
created to tackle this issue by providing a ordable, scalable
solutions. The project aims to screen 40 million eyes and
provide eyeglasses to 5 million people over ve years. This
initiative combines technolo y with local entrepreneurship,
ensuring that communities not only receive vision care but
also bene t from job creation and economic development.

Innovative Technolo y and Approach
The key innovation in the Chashma Project is the use of
portable autorefractors, devices that can diagnose
refractive errors in just 20 seconds. These devices are userfriendly and require minimal training, enabling local
healthcare workers to conduct vision screenings in even
the most remote areas. The autorefractors are highly
accurate and e cient, allowing up to 50,000 patients to be
screened per device each year.
Once a patient’s prescription is determined, eyeglasses
can be made locally for as little as USD 1 per pair, using
durable materials and a simple manufacturing process. By
involving local communities in the production and
distribution of eyeglasses, Manu’s vision care initiative not
only addresses a critical health need but also fosters local
entrepreneurship.

fi

ff

g

g

ff

ffi

fi

665

Partnerships and Progress
To implement this vision, Manu has partnered with large
scale nonpro t organizations, which have extensive
experience in healthcare delivery. These partnerships
ensure that the initiative reaches the people who need it
most. The Chashma Project has already screened 100,000
individuals in its initial phase, with 25 30% of those
screened requiring eyeglasses. Additionally, the project has
referred thousands of patients for cataract surgeries,
further expanding its impact.
With plans to scale up to 1 million eye examinations in
2023 alone, the Chashma Project is well on its way to
meeting its ambitious goals. The combination of advanced
technolo y, loc al par tnerships, and communit y
involvement is key to its success.

Anemia: Tackling a Silent Crisis
Anemia is a widespread health
issue, a ecting over 600 million
people worldwide. In India,
more than half of women aged
15 49 are anemic, a condition
that not only impacts individual
health but also undermines
economic productivity. Anemia
is primarily caused by iron
de cienc y, but it is often

-

fi

g

ff

fi

-

666

exacerbated by poor nutrition, lack of healthcare access,
and low awareness about the condition.
Manu’s personal experience with anemia has fueled his
passion for addressing this issue on a large scale. He
recognizes that while anemia is preventable and treatable,
millions of people continue to su er due to gaps in
detection and treatment. Manu’s approach focuses on
scalable, non-invasive technologies for early detection,
combined with education and nutritional support.

Cutting-Edge Detection Technologies
To address the anemia crisis, Manu has pioneered the
use of non-invasive technologies that can detect anemia
through simple, accessible methods. One of these
technologies uses smartphone cameras to analyze the color
of a patient’s inner eyelid, providing an accurate
assessment of hemoglobin levels without the need for
invasive blood tests. Another approach involves using
photos of patients’ ngernails to detect low hemoglobin
levels. Both technologies are designed to be used in
resource-limited settings, where traditional diagnostic tools
are unavailable.
These innovations make it possible to screen large
populations quickly and cost-e ectively, ensuring that
those at risk of anemia can be identi ed early and treated
appropriately. By integrating these technologies into
community health programs, Manu aims to screen 1 million
people for anemia over the next ve years.

Holistic Approach: Education and Nutrition

Through partnerships with organizations like Akshaya

and Ekal Vidyalaya, which focuses on education in rural
areas, Manu is integrating anemia prevention into existing
programs.
By raising awareness about the importance of a balanced
diet, particularly iron-rich foods, Manu’s initiative is
helping to combat the root causes of anemia. In addition,
the program provides iron supplements and nutritional
support to those most at risk, particularly women and
children. The goal is to reduce anemia rates by 20% in the
target populations over the next ve years.

Manu’s approach to healthcare is grounded in a theory of

Shaping the Future through

Vision to Solve the Problem of Quality

Manu’s vision for education extends beyond traditional

This initiative not only transformed the way students in

attention of global giants like Google, which followed
SMFT’s lead and contributed signi cant funds to support
Pratham’s tablet initiative. Today, Pratham’s digital content,

4.6
DA F vs P r iva te Fa m i ly
Fo u n d a t i o n vs P u b l i c
Foundation
Manu and Rika Shah have woven philanthropy into their lives
and business through charitable vehicles such as Donor-Advised
Funds (DAFs), the Sarva Mangal Family Trust (SMFT), and the Shah
Happiness Foundation (SHF). Beginning with a DAF in 1995, they
structured their giving to maximize impact, eventually expanding
their e orts through SMFT, which also fosters charitable
involvement among MSI employees. In 2022, they launched SHF to
ensure the continuation of their philanthropic legacy, driven by
their belief in purposeful, long-term giving.

ff

701

hilanthropy encompasses various structures and
strategies for charitable giving. Let's break down the
three types of philanthropic vehicles you've mentioned:
Donor-Advised Funds (DAFs), private family foundations,
and public foundations.

Donor-Advised Funds (DAFs)
A DAF is a charitable giving vehicle administered by a
public charity. It allows donors to make a charitable
contribution, receive an immediate tax deduction, and
then recommend grants from the fund over time.
Advantages:
• Immediate Tax Deduction: Donors get an immediate
tax deduction for contributions, even if grants are made
in the future.

702

• Simplicity and Convenience: DAFs are easy to set up
and administer. The sponsoring organization handles all
the administrative tasks.
• Flexibility: Donors can recommend grants to various
charities over time.
• Privacy: Donors can choose to remain anonymous.
Disadvantages:
• Control: Donors have advisory privileges but not legal
control over the funds.
• Fees: There are management fees charged by the
sponsoring organization.
• Payout Requirements: There are no strict payout
requirements, which might delay funds reaching
charities.

Private Family Foundations
A private family foundation is a non-pro t organization
created and funded by an individual, family, or
corporation. It is usually governed by family members or
trustees.
Advantages:
• Control: Donors have full control over the foundation's
assets and grant-making decisions.
• Legacy: Foundations can be structured to continue for
generations, preserving the donor's legacy.
• Flexibility in Grant-making: Foundations can make
grants to individuals and international organizations,

fi

703

fund scholarships, and undertake other charitable
activities.
Disadvantages:
• Complexity and Cost: Setting up and maintaining a
private foundation involves signi cant legal, accounting,
and administrative work.
• Annual Distribution Requirement: Foundations must
distribute at least 5% of their assets annually.
• Public Disclosure: Foundations must le detailed
annual tax returns (Form 990 PF), which are publicly
available.

Public Foundations
Public foundations are non-pro t organizations that receive
funding from multiple sources, including individuals,
corporations, and government agencies. They typically
focus on speci c causes or communities.
Advantages:
• Broader Funding Base: Public foundations can attract
funds from a wide range of donors.
• Community Impact: They often have deep ties to
speci c communities or causes, allowing for targeted
impact.
• Professional Management: Public foundations typically
have professional sta to manage funds and grantmaking.

fi

fi

-

fi

ff

fi

fi

704

• Less Control for Individual Donors: Individual donors
have less control over how their contributions are used.
• Complexity: Managing diverse funding sources and
grant programs can be complex.
• Potential for Bureaucracy: Larger public foundations
might have more bureaucratic processes.

For Manu and Rika Shah, the spirit of giving has been an
integral part of their personal and professional lives. Their
philanthropic journey is not just about charity, but about
fostering a culture of generosity and impact within MSI and
beyond. This chapter delves into their charitable
endeavors, the foundations they’ve established, and their
commitment to meaningful, scalable philanthropy.

The Genesis of Giving: Donor-Advised Funds
Starting with DAF
In 1995, with a vision to make giving a structured part of
their life, Manu and Rika initiated a Donor-Advised Fund
(DAF) with Fidelity. Starting with an initial contribution of
$200,000, this fund was designed to simplify the giving
process, allowing them to contribute earnings and grow the
fund over time. This approach not only facilitated ongoing
donations without the immediate need to allocate
resources each year but also allowed them to strategically
plan their philanthropic activities.

Expanding the Reach: Sarva Mangal Family
Trust
SMFT Empowering MSI Employees and Beyond
In 2003, the Shahs took their commitment to
philanthropy further by establishing the Sarva Mangal
Family Trust (SMFT), translating to "Let All Be Happy." This
trust underscored their dedication not just to global causes

—

706

but also to their immediate MSI family. SMFT o ers
matching contributions up to $5,000 for every MSI
employee’s charitable donations, thereby extending the
culture of giving throughout the organization. This initiative
not only boosts the employees' morale but also ampli es
their impact on the charities they care about.

Fostering a Culture of Volunteerism and
Support
Encouraging Active Participation
Beyond nancial contributions, SMFT encourages a
culture of active volunteerism within MSI. Through
competitions and art from these events, the trust promotes
engagement and raises additional funds for charitable
causes. To date, SMFT supports over 40 non-pro t
organizations worldwide, often as a prime contributor or a
signi cant supporter. These organizations are carefully
selected based on their scalability, measurability, and
transparency to ensure that the contributions have the
most meaningful impact.

The Shah Happiness Foundation: Securing the
Future of Philanthropy
A New Chapter in Charitable Giving
In 2022, the Shahs expanded their philanthropic
framework by establishing the Shah Happiness Foundation
(SHF). With four full-time employees and growing, SHF
represents a strategic approach to long-term charitable

giving. Through this foundation and their estate planning,
the Shahs are ensuring that their philanthropic vision will
continue to in uence and enact positive change, adhering
to Manu’s philosophy that the "last check should bounce,"
re ecting a life spent giving to the fullest.

Conclusion: The Ripple E ects of Generous
Leadership
The philanthropic initiatives started by Manu and Rika
